PLEASANT DIALOGVES AND DRAMMA'S, SELECTED OVT OF LUCIAN, ERASMUS, TEXTOR,
OVID, etc. With sundry Emblems extracted from the most elegant Iacobus Catsius.
As also certain Elegies, Epitaphs, and Epithalamions or Nuptial Songs;
Anagrams and Acrosticks; With diverse Speeches (upon several occasions) spoken
to their most Excellent Majesty's, King CHARLES, and Queen MARY. With other
Fancies translated from BEZA, BUCANAN, and sundry Italian Poets.

By THO. HEYWOOD.

Aut prodesse solent, aut delectare --

LONDON, Printed by R. O. for R. H. and are to be sold by Thomas Slater at the
Swan in Duck-lane 1637.

To the Right Honorable Sir HENRY Lord CARRY, Baron of Hunsdon, Viscount
Rochford, Earl of DOVER, etc.
Right Honorable,
ELaborate Poems have ever aimed at learned Patrons, who valued Books as your
best Lapidaries praise Jewels, not by their greatness, but their goodness.
This is a small Cabinet of many and choice, of which none better than your
Noble self can judge, some of them borrowing their luster from your own
virtues, vouchsafe therefore (great Lord) their perusal, being devoted to our
sole patronage, whilst the presenter wishing unto you and all yours, a long
fruition of terre^riall graces here, with the fullness of celestial ^yes
hereafter, humbly takes his leave, with that of Catullus to M. Cicero:

Tanto pessimus omnium poeta,
Quanto tu optimus omnium patronus.
Your Lordships in all dutiful observance, THO. HEYWOOD.

To the Generous Reader.
REader, of what capacity or condition soever, I present unto thy favorable
perusal a Miscellane of sundry strains in Poetry; which me thinks should not
come altogether unwelcome to such as affect variety: here thou shall find
choice and selected Dialogues borrowed from sundry Authors, both for the method
and matter, pleasant and profitable. Which though I met with in Prose only,
yet upon better acquaintance, I have taught to go upon even feet and number.

For such as delight in Stage-poetry, here are also diverse Dramma's, never
before published: Which though some may condemn for their shortness, others
again will commend for their sweetness.

From famous Iacobus Catsius, I have extracted Emblems of risk conceit, and
excellent expression in the original; Therefore I hope not to be rejected in
our native Tongue, howsoever by me but rudely and coursely interpreted.

Here are moreover diverse speeches, at sundry times, and upon several
occasions spoken, either to one or both of their sacred Majesty's. And other of
the same condition, before other Noble Personages.

Besides Epithalamions (or Nuptial Songs) with Funeral Elegies, Epitaphs,
Anagrams, etc. Nor doubt I, but in the service of such change of dishes, there
may be found amongst them, though not all to please every man, yet not any of
them but may taste some one or others palate. For the better illustration of
which. I have prefixed before every particular piece its proper Argument, with
Annotations and observations of all such things as may appear difficult or
foreign to the ignorant Reader. Which I entreat thee to accept as well in
plain iake, as were they curiously insculpt in Copper. Complement I cannot to
only that I take my ^eave; Reader farewell. Read perfectly, examine strictly,
but censure charitably.

Thine, THO. HEYWOOD.

To his worthy friend the Author, Master Thomas Heywood.
HEywood, when men weigh truly what thou art,
How the whole frame of learning claims a part
In thy deep apprehension; and then see;
To knowledge added so much industry;
Who will deny thee the best Palm and Bays?
And that to name thee, to himself is praise.
As first, which I must ever first prefer,
Thy skill in Poetry, where thou so far
Have gone, as none beyond thee, and have writ,
That after-ages must despair of wit
Or matter to write more. Nor art thou less,
In whatsoere thy fancy will express.
Thy pen commands all history, all actions,
Counsels, Decrees, men, manners, States, and factions,
Plays, Epicediums, Odes, and Lyricks,
Translations, Epitaphs, and Panegyricks:
They all do speak thy worth. Nor do thou teach
Things mere profane; but thy great Muse does reach
Above the Orbs; unto the utmost sky,
And makes transition unto Deity.
When thou with such high strains detainst our ears,
As might become the Angels, or the Spheres.
What Reader then in justice can decline
From this assertion? Poets are divine,
Rapt with a heavenly fire, which is made known
By no example better than thine own.
SH. MARMION.

To the learned Author Master THOMAS HEYWOOD.
Who can deny but Poets take their birth
From some thing that is more excellent than earth
Since those harmonious strains that fill our ears,
Proclaim their near alliance with the Spheres,
And shows their Art all Arts as far exceed
As does the fiery-Cane, the weakest Reed.
That Matter which six lines of Prose rehearse,
May fitly be contained in one Verse;
Yea, and so pithily (if well compacted)
That out of it whole Books may be extracted.
A President whereof if thou would find,
I prithee gentle Reader bend thy mind
To what this little Volume does contain,
And sure the fruit will recompense thy pain.
The subject with the Authors names agree,
Who all have left unto Posterity
Such Noble badges of their learned fame,
That my weak Pen can no way show the same;
Therefore do thou, o Heywood, wear the Bays
As thy just merit many thousand ways.
For this thy Work, with others heretofore
Shall honor thee till time shall be no more.
D. E.

To my praiseworthy friend Master THOMAS HEYWOOD.
THy Worth unto the Knowing World is known,
Let Critics censure others by their own,
And tinct their foreheads with a purple shame,
When they shall see thy Works, or hear thy Name,
Whilst with thy own, thou setst forth others fame;
Whose lofty Anthems, in our English tone
Thou sing'st, and make them live, though dead and gone.
What barking or untutored Momus then
Will dare to belch against thy learned Pen?
Whose worthier Lines, unto their foul disgrace,
Shall spit defiance in a brazen face;
And when thou art dead, thy Poe^sie shall sing
Such pleasant strains, whereof the World shall ring;
And Envies self, in spite of all Assayes,
Shall crown thy Tombe-stone with eternal Bays.
S. N.

Tabulae Finis.
The Argument of Erasmus his Dialogue called NAIAGAION, or Naufragium.
HEre you may read an accurate Narration
Of dangers incident to Navigation:
With diverse foolish superstitions used
^y Mariners, (some not to be excused)
Here is described a Tempest to the height,
With casting out of Goods, to case their freight;
And several humors (to the life expressed)
Of men in danger, and by sea distressed:
^ome, to the blessed Virgin call for aid:
^y others, Vows to several Saints are made.
But this our Author will approve of none
To be invok't, but the Great God alone.
The Interlocutors or Speakers, Antonius and Adolphos. The DIALOGUE.
Anthon. THou tel'st me wondrous things; Is that to sail,
Where humane help so little can prevail?
Forbid it Heaven, to come into my thought,
That ever Wit so dearly should be bought.

Adol. What has as yet been spoke are trifles mere,
If to what I shall speak thou lend an ear.

Anth. So much from thee I have already had,
That I still tremble, and it makes me sad,
As I had then been present.

Adol. Dangers past
Are unto me of much more pleasing taste:
That night there happened what much took away
All comfort from the Pilot.

Anth. What, I pray?

Adol. Dark was the night; when by the topmast stand
(Got thither by the help of feet and hands)
One of the ship-men, and as from a It is commonly called the Bowland.
loover
He looked from thence, if so he might discover
Some part of land: when on the instant, near
Unto his side was seen a fiery Sphere;
To Seamen a sad Omen, if it shine
Single: but twins, they better luck divine:
And in the times of old they called such too
Castor and Pollux.

Anth. What had they to do
With Mariners? since those we understand
Were Champions both, and used to fight on land.

Adol. The Poets so would have it. He at the stern
Casting his eye up did the light discern:
Who calling said, My Mate (It is a word
That Sailers interchangeably afford
To one another) speak, do thou not see
The fire above that clings so close to thee?
Who answered thus: I do, and I pray God
That unto us it no misfortune boad.
The flaming Globe straight by the tackles slid,
And came close to the Pilot.

Anth. I! But did
Not he sink down with fear?

Adol. The fright he' endured,
They being to such prodigies inured.
There having staid a while, by the ship sides
It rowles it self, but there not long abides,
But leaping from the hatches, vanished so.
Toward midday the tempest 'gan to grow
More and more raging. Did thou ever see
The Alps?

Anth. I have.

Adol. Those hills appear to be
But warts to such sea billows, (if compared:)
Be judge then, how with us it that time fared;
How often were we lifted up so high,
Till to the very Moon we came so nigh,
To touch it with our fingers. Then again
So low cast, that the Channel rent in twain,
To let us down to Hell.

Anth. Mad men, no doubt,
Who leave the land, to seek such dangers out.

Adol. The Sailers striving with the Storm some space,
(But all in vain) the Pilot with a face
Like ashes, came to us.

Anth. And now I fear,
By his wan color, some strange mischief near.

Adol. I am no more your Pilot now (says he)
My friends, the Winds command both ship and me:
Prepare for all extremes, there is now no hope
Save in our God, no trust in Sail or Rope.

Anth. ('It Was an hard speech.)

Adol. First therefore let us ease
Our ship (says he) by casting in the seas
Her weighty lading; for so now commands
Necessity: It with more safety stands,
By loss of goods, death present to prevent,
Than with them perish here incontinent.
The truth persuades them; Instantly they hoise
unto the Maine, rich Wares, and Vessels choice,
And those in plenty.

Anth. This a Wrack indeed
May well be called.

Adol. Silence till I proceed.
Amongst the rest, a rich Italian there,
Imployd in Embassy, who was to bear
Some Presents into Scotland, and this Lord
Had coffers, caskets, and stuffed trunks abord,
With plate, rings, Jewels, change of garments.

Anth. Say,
Was that man willing to cast all away?

Adol. No: but being asked that question, made reply,
He with his wealth would live, or with it die;
And therefore stormed.

Anth. What said the Pilot then?

Adol. Better it were, of these despairing men,
That he alone should perish, than (to save
His proper wealth) all suffer in the wave:
And therefore told him plainly, But if he
Unto the general safety would agree,
(Need so compeld) that without further plea,
Him and his wealth they would toss into the sea.

Anth. A very Sailers speech.

Adol. So, forced at last,
With his own hands his goods away he cast,
With many bitter curses; much enraged
With gods and devils, that he had engaged
Himself to such a barbarous element.

Anth. A mere Italians prayer.

Adol. Observe the event:
(These our free-offrings notwithstanding) neither
The winds nor waves were sated, but together
Conspired: Our tackles were asunder blown,
And our torn sails into the Ocean thrown,

Anth. Distress indeed.

Adol. The Pilot comes again.

Anth. To preach as at the first?

Adol. In a sad strain
He thus salutes us: Friends, as the case stands,
I wish you would commend you to heavens hands,
And so prepare for death. Some who had been
At sea before, and in that Art well seen,
Asked him, How long he thought he could maintain
His ship to live? who briefly said again,
Not full three hours, (as being then at worst)

Anth. Why this was harder doctrine than the first.

Adol. Which having said, the Sailers he straight bid
To cut the cords asunder: which they did.
And next, To saw the Mainmast by the root:
Who instantly apply themselves vntoo't;
Which, with the sail and saile-yard, they soon threw
Into the sea.

Anth. Why so?

Adol. Because they knew,
Bee'ng torn, a burden they might rather call
Their sails, than help, (now of no use at all)
For all their hope was in the helm,

Anth. Mean space,
What did the passengers?

Adol. A wretched face
Of things you now might see: Some then in place
Began to sing, Hail Mary full of Grace;
And the blessed Virgin Mother to implore:
She, who plain Mary had been called before,
They now style, The Seas Star, The Queen of heaven,
The Lady of the world: Titles not given
To her in sacred Scriptures.

Anth. I indeed
Never that she at sea was yet could reed.

Adol. But Venus (I have heard) once took no scorn
To have the charge of Sailers, (as sea-borne.)
But thinking she had quite given up her care;
All their Devotions now directed are
In stead of her, a mother, and no maid,
Her that was Maid and Mother, to persuade.

Anth. Come now you jest.

Adol. Some of them prostrat lie
Upon the hatches, and for succor cry
Unto the Storm, and (as had they been mad)
poured out into the Maine what oil they had;
Flattring the raging billows of the seas,
As if some angry power they would appease.

Anth. What did they say?

Adol. O Sea most merciful,
O generous Sea, o Sea most beautiful,
O you the most rich Channels of the Deep
Save us, have mercy, us preserve and keep.

Anth. Ridiculous superstition. What the rest?

Adol. Their stomachs some disgorg'd; one in his breast
Was meditating Vows. An English man
(I well remember) said, O if I can
But get to land safe, Pilgrimage I Will frame
Unto the blessed Maid of Walsinghame;
And promised golden mountains. Others vowed
To such a Cross: but that some disallowed.
And named another in a remoat place
Thence many countries distant. In like case
They with the Virgin Mary dealt, who reigns
In sundry Regions: and since need constraines,
They pray to her, but think they are not heard,
Unless they name some Temple to her reared.

Anth. Vain were such Orisons, since the Saints dwell
In heaven above.

Adol. Some said, If they came well
And safe to shore, Carthusians they would be.
One promised, If the sea he once could free,
Bare foot and bare head, naked save his shirt,
And that of male close to his body girt,
Nay, begging all the way, vowed, steps he would tell
To where Saint James yet lives in Compostell.

Anth. Did none think of Saint Christopher?

Adol. I heard
Not without laughter) one to him indear'd:
He in the chief Church of Or Pari
Lutetia stands,
More like a mountain than a man) his hands
Lift up: who with a voice strep'rous and loud
That all they in the ship might hear him) vowed
To set before that Saint a waxen Light
Big as himself. To whom one that fore-right
Before him sat, (well known to him) replied,
After he first had jogged him on the side)
Take heed friend what you promise; should you sell
Your whole estate, which is to me known well,
You cannot make it good. He then in fear,
Lest him perchance S. Christopher might heart)
Answered in a low voice, Peace fool be still,
Think thou my words are suting to my will;
^f once I find safe landing may be had,
I Will of a farthing candle make him glad.

Anth. O stupid brain! Some Hollander?

Adol. None such:
He was of Zeeland sure.

Anth. I wonder much,
None that time of the Apostle Paul did think;
For he was wrackt, and when the ship did sink,
Got to the shore) who knowing shipwreck best,
Would soon have helped them in that kind distressed.

Adol. Of him there was no mention.

Anth. Did they pray?

Adol. Yes; and at once some sung, and some did say
Hail Virgin: others, their Belief: some muttered
Certain peculiar prayers, as had they vtter'd
Soft Magic spells' against danger.

Anth. How distress
Makes men devout? when they think nothing less
Than of their God, if fortune seem to smile,
Or of his Saints. But what did thou the while?
Vowd'st thou to none of them?

Adol. No.

Anth. Why?

Adol. Because
Cov'nants with Saints made, are still with some clause
After the form of Contract: This I give,
If thou perform: If at this time I live,
Then such a thing I Will do; I Will at thy Shrine
Offer a Taper, if I escape the Brine;
Or if thou keepst me, unto Rome I Will go
On Pilgrimage.

Anth. But to none prayd'st thou?

Adol. No.

Anth. Show me the cause?

Adol. I thought, Heaven far extended:
To any one Saint should I have commended
My safety, say Saint Peter, who bee'ng near
Unto the door, most likely was to hear;
Before he could have left the gate, to find
where God was, or delivered him my mind,
I might have perished.

Anth. What then did thou do?

Adol. Took the next course, and did direct unto
The Father my Devotions, and began,
Father which art in heaven, etc. I perceived than,
None of the Saints could sooner hear, nor any
Abler to save or help, though they be many.

Anth. Did not thy conscience prick thee the mean time
Remembering with how many an heinous crime
Thou had offended him?

Adol. Shall I speak true?
Part of my confident boldness it withdrew;
But straight it thus in my conception run:
No Father is so angry with his Son,
But if he spy him in a brook or lake,
Ready to drown, he will by the hair him take,
And pluck him from the danger. Amongst the rest,
A woman who a child had at her breast
Then sucking, in that fear seemed troubled least.

Anth. And what did she?

Adol. Nor clamor loud, nor weep;
Nor promise what she never meant to keep:
Only embraced her infant, softly prayed
Unto her self, none hearing what she said.
Mean time the Bark inclining near the shore,
The Master fearing lest she would be tore
And split to pieces; her with cables bound
From helm to the fore-decke.

Anth. Comfort unsound.

Adol. Up then a sacrificing Priest arose,
Aged sixty years, through doublet and through hose
His torn shirt seen, (called Adam) who his shoes
That had no soles) cast off, and 'gins to unloose
His wretched habit; bidding all prepare
Themselves to swim, who of their lives had care.
And standing on the deck, begins to preach
Alowd to us, and out of Gerson teach
Five truths; what profit from Confession grows,
Wishing we would make ready to dispose
Our selves to life or death. Then present there
Was a Dominican Friar of look austere,
To whom some few confessed themselves.

Anth. But what
did thou mean space?

Adol. I well perceiving, that
All things were full of tumult, soon confessed
My self to God, against whom I had transgrest;
Blaming mine own injustice, and commended
My self to him, whom I had most offended.

Anth. Had thou then perished, whither had thou gone?

Adol. That I committed unto God alone,
As most unwilling mine own judge to be:
And yet a fair hope did still comfort me.
Whilst these things past, the Pilot came again,
With his cies full of tears, and says, In vain
We strive against heaven: each man himself prepare;
The shaken ship in which distressed we are
Cannot the fourth part of an hour well last,
At sundry leaks the water poures so fast.
Soon after he brings news he did descry
A Chapel afar off: bids us apply
Our prayers, the small space that the ship still floated,
Unto that Saint to whom it was devoted:
When suddenly most part are groueling thrown,
Devoutly praying to the Saint unknown.

Anth. Had they but named him, he would sure have heard

Adol. But that they knew not. Then the Pilot steard
His torn ship that way, ready now to sink,
(Such quantity of water forced to drink)
And split she had in pieces in that weather,
Had not the cables bound her fast together.

Anth. 'Twas an hard case.

Adol. It drawing now toward even,
Upon the sudden we so far were driven
Toward the coast, that us the inhabitants spy'de,
And seeing our extremes, called out and cry'de;
And with their hats upon their staves end, stand
Pointing to us the safest place to land:
Then with their arms stretched out, seem to deplore
Our wretched case, distressed so near the shore.

Anth. I long to know what happened.

Adol. Our Bark now
Had took in so much water, that I vow
There hardly any diffrence could be known,
Because the ship and sea appeared all one.

Anth. To the holy Anchor it was time to fly.

Adol. And yet small comfort, seeing death so nigh.
The Sailers hoise the boat, and let it down
^^to the Sea: then there is a tumult grown,
^ho should press soonest in. Some began to exclaim,
^rying, Why throng you thus? Be ruled for shame;
^he Boat's but small, and were you not thus rude,
^ncapable of such a multitude.
They bid them search, and what came nearest, get
^o save themselves. When now there was no let,
^ut every one, that which came next him snatches:
One lights upon a piece of the torn hatches:
^n empty barrel he: another takes
^ planke: that man a pole: and none but makes
^ome shift or other: so themselves commit
unto the sea.

Anth. You have not told me yet,
What of the woman and the child became,
^he only that was heard not to exclaim.

Adol. She got to shore first.

Anth. Tell me how that past?

Adol. Her to a crooked planke we ty'de so fast,
That hardly she could slide thence: in whose hand
We put a board (such as she might command)
^n stead of a small oar: then having prayed
^or her success, as she was thereon laid,
Exposed her to the waves, and with a spear,
Thrust her from off the ship, which now was near
Hid in the sea, her infant she bestowed
^n her left arm, and with her right hand rowed.

Anth. A stout Virago.

Adol. When naught else remained,
One snatcheth an old Image, blur'd and stained,
Part of it eat with rats, which once presented
The mother Virgin: and with that contented,
Begins to swim.

Anth. But came the boat to shore?

Adol. They were the first that perished, none before;
For thirty had therein together got.

Anth. By what ill chance was that?

Adol. 'Twas their hard lot;
For e're they from the ship themselves could free,
The weak boat split, and sunk immediately.

Anth. A sad disaster: But what then?

Adol. I cherisht
Others, and had my self like to have perished.

Anth. As how?

Adol. I stayed till nothing did appear
Helpful to swim.

Anth. Cork had been useful there.

Adol. I tell thee Friend, just at that instant space
I Would rather had a Cork tree to embrace,
Than a rich golden Candlestick. About
Looking, to spy what best I could find out,
I soon bethought me of the poor remain
Of the split Mast, at which I tugged in vain;
And therefore called an helper. We combine
Our double strength, and both to it incline,
Trusting our selves to sea; and in that fright
He by the left part holds: I take the right.
Thus by the billows tossed, the Predicant, whom
I named before, just at our backs did come,
And threw himself upon us: like an hulke
To us he seemed, being of a mighty bulk.
Wherewith much troubled, both aloud 'gan call,
Who is that third who means to drown us all?
He gently us bespake, and bad us be
Of comfort, there was room enough for three.

Anth. But wherefore did he leave the ship so late?

Adol. He purposed in the boat to try his fate
With the Dominican Friar; the rest to grace
Their Orders, willing to afford them place.
But though they both were in the ship confessed,
Belike forgetting some word amongst the rest,
They fell to it again, and somewhat s'ed
^aying ones hand upon the others head:
^eane time the boat sunk, by the waves controld:
For so much, after, to me Adam told.)

Anth. But what of the Dominican became?
^e, first invoking sundry Saints by name.
So Adam said) did strip himself to the skin;
And having left his clothes behind, leapt in.

Anth. What Saints did he invoke?
^e named (thick,
^s fast as he could speak) S. Dominick,
^aint Thomas, and Saint Vincent, and one Peter,
I know not which) but one she-Saint, with sweeter
and fairer words hee'ntreated; and her name,
^atherine Senensis, she, it seemed, the same
To whom he trusted most.

Anth. I, but Christ's aid
^mplor'd he not at all?

Adol. So the Priest said.

Anth. Me thinks he better might have fared that day,
Had he not cast his holy hood away.
For being naked like another man,
^ow could the Saint know the Dominican?
Touching thy self proceed.

Adol. Whilst we were tossed
Near to the bark, still fearing to be lost,
Part of the stern then floating, burst his thigh,
Who held the left part of the mast, whilst I
Made good the right: who soon his hold lets slip,
And so was drowned. Into whose place does skip
Adam the Priest, repeating a short prayer
That his soul (then departing) well might fare;
Exhorting me to be of courage bold,
^tretch out my legs, and with my hands keep hold:
Mean time we drunk much brine out of the Ocean,
It Was not a salt bath only, but salt potion.
(So Neptune then would have it) for which he
(Adam I mean) would show a remedy.

Anth. And what was that?

Adol. Still as he spy'de the wave
To come upon us, he himself to save,
Opposed it with the hind part of his head,
Keeping his mouth fast shut.

Anth. I never read
Of a more stout old fellow.

Adol. Floating long,
And moving somewhat onward, he bee'ng strong,
And wondrous tall, says to me, Be of cheer,
For by my foot I find the ground is near.
But I that time more timorous and afraid,
(Hoping no such good fortune) to him said,
Most certain we are farther from the shore,
Than to hope land. He now encouraged more,
Says to me, With my foot I touch the sand.
Perhaps, said I, some chest driven near the land,
Wrought thither by the sea. He affirms No,
And says, the ground he touches with his to.
We still were tossed, and he again feels shore:
Do what thou will (then said) for here no more
I Will trust my self, but toward land make have;
So farewell, for I Will leave thee to thy mast.
Then watching when the wave began to break,
With speed pursues it, and no more would speak:
But as the billow (shrinking back) he sees,
With either hand embracing both his knees,
He waits for it, drencht over head and ears,
(As Ducks or sea-Birds) and again appears
When the waue's past, and runs. Finding his fate
So well succeed, I thought to imitate
Him in his course: There stood upon the sands
Some people with long javelins in their hands,
Men strong and used to storms; these reached their sta^
To every faint hand that their succor craves.
^ho catching hold, some by that means they drew
^fe to the shore.

Anth. How many of that Crew?

Adol. Seven only, of which, two brought to the fire,
^ut feeling warmth, did instantly expire.

Anth. How many were in the ship?

Adol. Just fifty eight.

Anth. O cruel sea, to ruin such a freight.
I might with the tenths at most have been suffis'd,
^riests ask no more when they are best advised.
^ut of so great a number did so few
Escape the Wrack?

Adol. I speak it who best knew:
And there we found a remarkt approbation
Of a most generous and indulgent Nation;
Who with alacrity and much cheer gave
^arbor, meat, drink, with all things we could crave.

Anth. What country?

Adol. Holland.

Anth. None I take to be
More generous, fuller of humanity,
Though girt with barbarous countries. But I fear
Thou Wilt not to sea in haste.

Adol. Troth not this year,
No nor the next: I Will be no more such pray,
Unless (quite mad) Heaven take my wits away.

Anth. For such discourses I so little love them,
That I had rather hear them far, than prove them.

The Argument to Erasmus his Dialogue, entitled PROCVS and PVELLA.
ERASMUS in this Colloquie
Expresses what pure modesty
There ought to be 'twixt Man and Maid,
When there is a firm foundation laid
Of their affections. His intent
Was, how to leave a president,
All wanton Toys to intercept,
That chaste Vows might be made and kept.
As well the Prince as Peasant hence
May take advice of consequence.
It shows how true Love should be placed,
Forbidding Marriage made in have:
And that the Choice is not confined
Unto the Body, but the Mind.
His Project further does imply
The honor of the Nuptial Ty,
Which is not lawful to proceed
Before the Parents first agreed.
Of the sincere alternate life
Which ought to be 'twixt Man and Wife.
Next, how their Children should be bred,
As both by good Example led,
And Precept taught. What joy, what care
The Good and Bad to Parents are.
Wedlock with Single life compared,
I, and preferred in some regard.
That in the choice of any Bride
'T is Reason ought to be the Guide,
And not Affection. Here Is commixt
Sport, with Philosophy: betwixt,
Various discourse. The matter's ground
Worthy an Author so renowned.
The Speakers, PAMPHILVS and MARY. The DIALOGVE.
Pam. Hail to thee, o thou Cruel, who can vant
Of nothing else save iron and Adamant.

Mar. Hail to thee too (at length) o Pamphilus,
^ow, and as often as thou shall please: but thus
Therefore You should salute me, know I not,
^eemes to me my name you have forgot.
^^ria I am called.

(...). Had thou thy right,
^ou Martia had been named.

(...). I cannot fight,
Or know I what Mars means: Pray wherefore then
^^ke you me with that murderer of men?

(...). Because I hold thee more obdurat far,
^ thirsting blood, than is the god of War.
^kills for sport, (but such as he does hate)
^ thou thy Lovers, (Cruel and ingrate.)

(...). Good words I pray; to make me better skilled.
^v me the strage of those whom I have killed?
where is the blood?

(...). One Corse liuelesse and cold
^ou look upon when thou do me behold.

Mar. What do I hear? Did any ever know
A dead man (like thee) both to speak and go?
Should no more terrible Ghosts to me appear,
Trust me I never should be struck with fear.

Pa. Thou jest'st with me, and mean time strik'st me dead
And by degrees I Am hourly massacred,
Worse than if thou with steel should pierce my breast
For now with lingering death I am oppressed.

Mar. How many childing women with wet eyes
Were present to lament your obsequies?

Pam. And yet my palenesse argues (to my cost)
I am more bloudlesse than a walking Ghost.

Mar. And yet that palenesse has a violets hew:
You so look pale, as we in Summer view
The ripening Cherry, and your cheek is died
Like the Autumn Grape that is purpled on one side.

Pam. In sooth you do not well to jeere and flam
Me, knowing in what wretched case I am.

Mar. If thou beleev'st me not, there is a glass by,
Reach it, and that will speak as much as I.
No glass I wish, no Mirror can allow,
Save that in which I do behold me now.

Mar. What Mirror's that you speak of?

Pam. Your clear eyes.

Mar. You'ar the same Sophister, and still so wise
As you were ever: but I pray make it plain,
How you are liuelesse? and by me how slain?
Or is it the use of Shadows to take meat?

Pam. They do (like me) but taste not what they eat.

Mar. What is their food?

Pam. Leekes, Mallows, Pulse.

Mar. Indeed?
But sometimes you on Cock and Partridge feed.

Pam. But to my pallat are as much default
As should I feed on sallads without salt.

Mar. O miserable man! yet by this light
To me Y'appeare fat, fresh, and in good plight:
But can the Dead discourse?

Pam. Yes, they may speak,
But with a voice (like me) low, faint, and weak.

Mar. And yet (but lately) when revenge you vowed
Upon your Rival, you spoke shrill and loud.
But tell me further, as the Shadows talk,
Are they (like you) apparel'd? Can they walk?
Or do they sleep?

Pam. They do, such is their fate:
Nay more than that, sometimes subagitate
After their kind.

Mar. You trisle finely now:

Pam. But will you in your judgment yield and bow,
If it by Achillcan proofs be try'de,
That I am dead, and you the homicide?

Mar. Far be that Omen from us: But proceed
With that your Sophisme.

Pam. First then it is agreed,
Death's nothing but the absence of the Soul
From the frail body: (none can this control)
And that you will grant.

Mar. Well.

Pam. That which you agree,
You Will not recall hereafter.

Mar. 'T shall not be.

Pam. You Will not deny, That such as take a life
From any other, kill?

Mar. It Is without strife.

Pam. You Will likewise yield to that approved long since
By Authors, such as no man can convince,
Namely, That from the body the soul moves,
And is not where it lives, but where it loves.

Mar. Therein the advantage you of me have got;
Pray make it more plain, I understand it not.

Pam. In that I Am most unhappy, since I see
You are not alike sensible with me.

Mar. Then make me so.

Pam. You might with like pretense
Bid me to teach the Adamant to have sense.

Mar. I am a Maid, not stone.

Pam. And yet most sure,
Than the hardest Adamant you are more obdure,

Mar. Well, recollect your self.

Pam. (Though to be' admired)
All that with divine Raptures are inspired.
It Is said, nor hear, nor smell, nor see, nor feel,
Although you wound them with transpiercing steel.

Mar. So I have heard.

Pam. Know you the cause?

Mar. Not I:
Explain it you who read Philosophy.

Pam. Because the Soul's in heaven, when it does affect,
And absent from the flesh in that respect.

Mar. What then?

Pam. What then? thou Cruel? why this makes it plain,
Thou art the Murdresse: I the man new slain.

Mar. Where Is then thy soul?

Pam. Why where it loves?

Mar. But who
Has took it from thee? Wherefore sigh'sts thou so?
Speak freely, and unchecked?

Pam. One cruel, yet
She whom in death I never shall forget.

Mar. Y' are witty: But (my rare Philosopher)
Why likewise take you not a soul from her,
Repaying like with like?

Pam. Nor think it strange;
Nothing could prove more happy than such change,
And make me more essentially blessed,
Then mine in hers, if hers in mine would rest.

Mar. Shall I have leave (as thou but late with me)
That I may play the Sophister with thee?

Pam. The Sophistresse.

Mar. Can it with probability be sed,
That the same body is alive and dead?

Pam. But not at the same time.

Mar. The soul confine,
The body's dead, nor can thou call it thine.

Pam. I grant.

Mar. Nor quickens but when it is in place.

Pam. Well, be it so.

Mar. Speak then, how stands the case?
That being where it lives, in former state,
It keeps the body, whence it shifted late;
Or where it elsewhere lives, if it give breath,
How can it (whilst it lives) be taxt of death?

Pam. In Sophistry I see well skilled you are,
Yet can I easily euade this snare,
The Soul which does the living body sway,
^nproperly (me thinks) title you may
^ soul, when those that do the men control,
^re truly some small relics of the soul,
and nothing else. As when you take a Rose,
and smell to it, howeuer you dispose
^f the flower after: being gone again,
^he sent thereof will on your hand remain,

Mar. I see they only shall lost labor win,
^ho seek to catch an old Fox in a gin:
^t there is one thing more that I demand,
and I from you would gladly understand;
^^th not he act, that is staind with murders gilt?
^d suffer not all such whose bloods are spilt?

Pam. Most true.

Mar. How comes it then, when as the Wooer
This case may be said to be the doer,
^^ she that is wooed, the Patient (which is plain,
^^ stiffely to oppose it were but vain)
that is beloved, no such intent pursuing,
Should not be that? he cause of his own ruin.

Pam. Quite contrary: he (we see daily proved)
Suffer, who loves: she acts that is beloved.

Mar. The (a) Areopagitae (Grammar-skil'd)
In this cannot evince me.

Pam. You Are selfe-will'd:
Yet shall (b) the Amphictriones by Logic do it.

Mar. There Is one doubt, prithee answer me untoo't;
Whether is this your love free, or constrained?

Pam. Most willingly I love, though thus disdained.

Mar. Since not to love, men likewise have free will,
Who ever loves, does aim himself to kill:
And the indictment well against him laid,
'Twere great injustice to accuse the Maid.

Pam. She is not said the Lover to have slain,
Because beloved, but not to love again.
For all such persons may be said to slay,
Who can preserve, and will not when they may.

Mar. Say a young man unlawfully should dote
Upon a Vestall, from the world remote;
Or cast his eye upon another wife:
Must these lie prostrat, to preserve his life?

Pam. But where this young man his affection vows,
The act both Law and Piety allows,
And yet is slain. But if that murder he
A sin that does appear so slight to thee.
I can of Witchcraft challenge thoe.

Mar. O fie!
Witchcraft? Forbid it you blessed Powers on hye:
Would thou make me a Circes?

Pam. I divine,
Thou art worse far, because a Bear or Swine
I Would rather be, than as thou see me now,
Senseless and without life.

Mar. Pray tell me how,
Or by what kind of Witchcraft do I kill?

Pam. By fascination.

Mar. Is it then thy will
^ turn my noxious eyes from thee?

Pam. Not so,
^ut rather let them still dwell here.

Mar. Fie no.
^ in mine eyes there be effascination,
^ow comes it there is no such alteration
^n others I behold? Now I divine,
^he witchcraft's not in mine eyes, but in thine.

Pam. Is It not enough, thy vowed friend to transperse,
^ut thou will still insult upon his hearse?

Mar. O pleasant dead man, that can talk so free:
^ut I pray speak, When shall thy funeral be?

Pam. Sooner than thou do deem, (I am afraid)
Unless thou suddenly afford'st me aid.

Mar. Can I work such a wonder?

Pam. Thou may do
A greater act, and with small labor too,
^estore the Dead to life.

Mar. Had I the weed
Called (c) Panaces.

Pam. Of Simples there is no need:
Only repay my love, that is void of lust,
(Than which, what thing more easy, or more just)
^here's nothing else can thee of murder clear.

Mar. But at what bar shall I be called to appear?
Before the Areopagitae?

Pam. No,
But at the bar of Venus.

Mar. Those that know
That goddess, say she is placable.

Pam. So ye'haue heard;
^ut there is none to be more dread and feared.

Mar. Carries she lightning?

Pam. Not.

Mar. Or does she bear
A Trident?

Pam. Neither.

Mar. Does she use a spear?

Pam. Not any: but she is goddess of the seas.

Mar. I do not use to sail.

Pam. But more than these;
She has a Boy.

Mar. His age can none affright.

Pam. But he is perverse, reuengefull, and of might.

Mar. What can he do to me?

Pam. What can he? All
The gods forbid, that you should prostrat fall
Beneath his fury: loath would I presage
Ill unto her, to whom my self I'engage.

Mar. I am not superstitious, speak thy mind.

Pam. I shall: If thou hereafter prov'st unkind,
Or shall appear so peevish or so fond
To one whose love with thine may correspond:
Should such a suit to Venus be commenced
By her the Boy would be so much insenc'd,
To aim a shaft in (e) Stiptick poison dipped,
By which thy hard breast on the sudden ripped,
It shall besot thee on some sordid Swain,
Which shall thy love repay with cold disdain.

Mar. An horrid punishment thou talkst of, I
A thousand times had rather wish to die,
Than perditly to'affect one base and vile,
And he his heart toward me not reconcile.

Pam. Yet of a Virgin subject to like fate
There has been known a sad example late.

Mar. What place?

Pam. Aurelia.

Mar. Since how many years?

Pam. How many months you would say, Still appear
The lamentable ruin, and the fame
I loud and frequent.

Mar. Speak, what was her name?
Why do thou pause?

Pam. I know her even as well
^s I do thee.

Mar. Then why do thou not tell
What her name is?

Pam. It Is for the Omens sake.
Which does not please me: I wish she could take
^ome other name upon her. You may gather
What hers is, by your own.

Mar. Who was her father?

Pam. A man of quality, and one that lives
amongst the Lawyers, unto whom he gives
No common luster.

Mar. I am now ambitious
^o know what his name is.

Pam. He Is called Mauritius:

Mar. But his syrname?

Pam. Aglaius.

Mar. Lives her mother?

Pam. No, but of late changed this life for another.

Mar. But of what sickness died she?

Pam. Would thou know?
^f sorrow, that her child was shipwrackt so.
^er father too, of valor proved and try'de,
^id little want but of conceit had died.

Mar. How was her mother styled, pray tell me true?

Pam. I will: Sopbronia: one that none but knew.
^ut what mean all these questions? do you think
speak a thing that is forged?

Mar. It cannot sink
^to my head: you rather may suspect
^ur fex for that, since fables we affect.
^t say, what happened to her then?

Pam. The Maid
Was borne in honest place, as I then said,
Of happy dower, and amiable feature:
Why should I hold you long? She was a creature
Fit for a Princes bed; and sought by one
Then every way her equal: there was none
More meriting.

Mar. How called?

Pam. The Omen does offend: yet thus
Receive his name, he was called Pamphilus:
Who though he proved all possible ways to win her,
Yet save disdain, when he found nothing in her,
Grief wasted him away: when she soon after
Doated upon a Groom composed for laughter;
Whom you might rather call an Ape than Man.

Mar. What is it you say?

Pam. So poorly, that I can
Scarce give thereof expression.

Mar. She so fair,
To dote on one deformed?

Pam. Thin his hair,
Besides, disorderd and vnkembd, his crown
Picked, made steeple-wise, and ouergrowne
With scurfe and dandruffe; bald he was beside,
Extremely squint-eyd, and his nosthrils wide
And bending upward, with a mouth most spacious,
His teeth both gagged and furred, his tongue ungracious
Stammering at every word; a scabbed chin,
And easily seen, because his beard was thin;
Crookt-backt, gow-bellied, bending at the knee
His legs.

Mar. Thersites thou describ'st to me.

Pam. Nay more; They say he has but one ear lost.

Mar. Perhaps the the other was in war bereft.

Pam. Most sure 'twas lost in peace.

Mar. Such an affront
What Is he durst give him?

Pam. Now I think upon it,
was the hangman.

Mar Notwithstanding this,
^^rhaps what in his feature is amiss,
^s substance may make good.

Pam. But he is no better
^^an a mere Bankrupt, one that is a debtor
^^ his own soul, and he has pawnd it often.
and yet she that is so tender, smooth, and soft,
^oth with this Monster bosom, drink, and eat;
^ay, at his churlish hands is often times beat.

Mar. A wretched tale, if truly understood.

Pam. And yet so Venus.
Nemesis has thought it good.
^ost true it is, nor could the goddess long
^efer due vengeance for the young man's wrong.

Mar. Than such a monster of a man to brook,
rather wish here to be thunder-strooke.

Pam. Then let not Nemesis be justly moved,
^rovoke her not, love where thou art belo'vd.

Mar. Would that suffice, with all my soul I Would do it.

Pam. Speak not the word, unless thou stand vntoo't.
^ wish moreover, That your love may be
^asting, and only proper unto me.
A wife, no mistress, I have now in chase.

Mar. I do not doubt it: yet in such a case,
When as our vows continue with our fate,
Behooues us long time to deliberate.

Pam. I have long enough considered.

Mar. Lest you err,
Take heed, for Love's but a bad Counselor,
And as they say, he is blind.

Pam. Blind love I scorn;
But that love sees, which is of judgment borne.
Thou do not therefore seem to me so fair,
Because I love thee; but I therefore dare
To love thee, since thou art as thou appear'st.

Mar. And yet beware how you esteem me dearest:
When you pull on your shoo you best may tell
In what part it does chiefly pinch you.

Pam. Well,
Dice must be cast for that, I and the rather,
Because by many Auguries I gather.
Things better may succeed.

Mar. An Augur too?

Pam. I am.

Mar. But what can your sooth-saying do?
Saw you the night-Crow fly?

Pam. 'Thad been in vain;
She only flies to such as have no brain.

Mar. Or did you see two Turtles take their flight
Either upon the left hand or the right?

Pam. Tush these are toys: yet one thing I have seen,
And long time marked; The goodness that has been
Derived unto you, nor does it foretell
Any bad Omen, to be borne so well;
Nor foreign unto me were their conditions,
Or with how many wholesome admonitions
Thy education from the first has bin,
With fair examples free from sight of sin.
"And better it is (the Dowrre to adorn)
"To have one well instructed, than well borne.
There is another Augurie beside:
My Ancestors (I speak it not in pride)
Are not of meanest rank, and in times past
With thine made league, which to this day does last.
And that, not vulgar, from our cradles we
Have known each other; but to disagree
Were never known: there is a parity
In our two years; in the nobility,
Riches, and honor of our parents. More,
(Which in this match I should have placed before)
Your sweet indowments and behauior rare
Did in all points with my condition square:
But whether mine with yours have suited well
In correspondence, that I cannot tell.
These are the Birds which I observed to fly,
Predicting only by their Augurie.
And these presage a marriage to ensue,
Happy and blessed, nay always seeming new.
Unless from your most delicate warbling throat
Should now proceed some harsh unpleasing noar
To erosse my hopes.

Mar. Say, What song do you wish?

Pam. I will begin, now answer you to this,
It Is but two words, and they soon learnd; I am thine:
Now echo unto me, and sing, Thou mine.

Mar. It Is a short song, and has as short a theme,
And yet it bears a long (f) Epiphoneme.

Pam. What matters it how long, so it be sweet.

Mar. And yet I should be loath, as we now meet,
That I to any motion should consent,
Of which perhaps in time you may repent.

Pam. O cease to boad us ill.

Mar. I may grow strange,
When age or sickness shall my beauty change.

Pam Crazed or in health, thou shall to me be one,
Equal in both, so dear unto me none.
gaze not on this building, rare and near;
The guest within I love.

Mar. What guest Pentreat?

(...). Thy mind, whose splendour with thy years does grow.

Mar. He had need of more than (g) Lynceus eyes, that so
Can through so many roofes at once espy.

Pam. Thy mind by mine I see perspicuously.
To add to these, we in our children may,
^s we wax old, grow younger every day.

Mar. I, but Virginity mean time is lost.

Pam. Tell me, if you your self had laid great cost
Upon an Orchard, you would think it sin,
Should nothing else but bare flowers grow therein:
Had you not rather (all the flowers bee'ng cropped)
To see the trees full branches underpropped,
Laden with ripe fruit?

Mar. O, you argue fine.

Pam. Or answer me: To see a drooping Vine
Fallen, and there putrefying where it is laid?
Or see one by her own kind claspings staid;
And round about some fair grown Elm to run,
Whilst her full clusters ripen against the Sun?
Which is the goodlier sight?

Mar. Now answer me:
Which of the two sights had you rather see:
A milk white Rose still shining in its thorn:
Or cropped, and in some durty bosom worn,
To lose her fair leaves?

Pam. As I understand,
That Rose is happier, gatherd by the hand,
And withers, after it does both delight
The nose with the sweet smell, the eye with sight.
Rather than that which gives no more content,
Than to the Brier forfeit both leaves and sent.
It grew for use, first to be gathered, then
To wither after So the wine that men
At merry meetings jovially down pour,
Is happier far, than what (vndrunke) grows sour.
Nor is the Virgin flower maturely grown,
Blasted as soon as cropped. Some I have known,
Before their marriage languish and look sickly,
Who after congresse have recovered quickly,
As if they had but then begun to spring.

Mar. And yet Virginity (you know's a thing)
Gracious and plausible to all.

Pam. It Is true,
Than a young Virgin, nothing to the view
More grateful: but what object can there be
^orse, than an old and wrinkled maid to see?
^nlesse thy mother had let fall her flower,
^hy blossom had not flourisht at this hour.
and if our future marriage (as I hope)
Do not prove barren, we shall then have scope,
Though that Virginity be lost and gone,
To yield the world a many for that one.

Mar. And yet pure chastitie's a thing (they say)
To God most grateful.

Pam. And I therefore pray,
He Will send me a chaste Virgin to my wife,
With whom to lead a chaste vnquestion'd life:
And by that means shall grow the greater Tie,
Of minds, then bodies; so shall you and I
Get to the public weal, to Christ beget,
Then how far distant is this wedlock set
From true Virginity: it may so fall,
That we in time may prove as conjugall
As Joseph lived with Mary. Mean time we
Shall practice 'twixt our selves a chastity,
To whose sublimitie none can come near,
Upon the sudden.

Mar. What is this I hear?
Must chastity be violated, and
Then after learnd?

Pam. What else? (Pray understand)
As when by drinking of a lesser draught,
We, by degrees, abstemiousnesse are taught:
In this affair with us so stands the state.
Which of the two hold you more temperate;
He at a full and furnished table placed,
And of no tempting delicate will taste;
Than he, removed from all that might accite,
Or any way provoke his appetite?

Mar. I hold him of a temperance far more great,
Who, when beset with dainties, will not eat.

Pam. In case of Chastity which stand you for?
Him that has made himself an Eunuch; or
One that is able bodied, strong, and sound,
And yet in whom there is no intemperance found?

Mar. Upon the last I dare bestow the Bays;
On the first, madness, and no other praise.

Pam. All such as by the strictness of their Vow,
No matrimonial Contract will allow,
What do they else but gueld themselves?

Mar. You say it.

Pam. It is not virtue, not to copulate.

Mar. How is it not?

Pam. Observe me: If it were
A virtue in itself, not to cohere;
It must be then a vice to have congresse.
But that to be most lawful we may guess,
By mutual consocietie. Again,
Marriage is honorable.

Mar. Make it more plain,
Why you infer this?

Pam. Since so often it falls:
As, to the loving wife the husband calls
For due benevolence; it only being
For issues sake.

Mar. But say there is disagreeing,
When it proceeds from wantonness and lust;
Then, to deny him, is it not right and just?

Pam. Rather admonish and entreat him fair;
That you may do: howeuer, bound you are
To yield to him, being instant. In that strain
Scarce hear I husbands of their wives complain.

Mar. But liberty is sweet.

Pam. Yet further hear;
Virginitie's a weighty load to bear.
But I thy King, and thou my Queen shall be;
We Will rule and reign in our own family:
^an that appear to thee a servitude?

Mar. But I have often heard marriage, by the rude
and Vulgar, called an Halter that fast ties.

Pam. All those that sacred marriage so despise,
^re of an halter worthy. This decide:
^^ not thy Mind unto thy Body ty'de?

Mar. It seems to be so.

Pam. Even just as you see
Bird incage'd; whom ask to be set free,
^e will deny't: and wherefore? Can you tell?
because her bondage does content her well.

Mar. Our means are but indifferent.

Pam. Therefore more
^^fe. The best way then to increase our store,
your good houswifery at home, whilst I
broad will use my utmost industry.

Mar. But many children still bring many cares.

Pam. And many pleasures too: I have known heirs,
or all the troubles and incessant fears,
^he cost and charge that in their tender years
^hey have put their parents to; being grown men,
^aue paid them back with double use again.

Mar. A miserable thing it were, I vow,
^o have had children, then to lose them.

Pam. Now,
^e you not childelesse? But at no good rate,
^ doubtful things thus ill you ominate.
which wish you rather to your lot might fall,
^ borne to die, or not be borne at all?

Mar. Why of the two, borne (as I am) to die,

Pam. So much more wretched is that Orbitie
^^d deprivation, which yet never had,
^ ever shall have issue; (to make glad)
they more happy are, borne to the earth,
^an they, nor borne, nor ever to have birth.

Mar. But who are they that are not, nor shall be?
Nay hear me yet a little further: He
Who humane frailties shall refuse to bear,
(To which even all men while they sojourne here,
Are equally obnoxious; keep the State,
Or be they low degreed) must yield to Fate.
But as for thee, let come what can betide;
For thou shall bear but half, I will divide
The burden with thee: nay, the greater share
I Will cast on mine own shoulders, (in my care)
But so, that in each joyful accident
Doubled shall be thy pleasure in the event.
If ought disastrous; my society may
Take (of the grief) the greatest part away:
And for your self (did but the Fates so please)
I wish on me no greater joy might sease,
Nor would I further happiness desire,
Than in thy sweet embraces to expire.

M. That which by Natures common course does chance
You men digest with easiest countenance.
But I see with some parents how it fares,
In whom their childrens manners breed more cares,
Than can their deaths.

Pam. But please you be content,
It lies in us that danger to prevent.

Mar. As how?

Pam. I Will make it plain; because we see
Never bad Fruit proceed from a good Tree,
As touching the condition, Nor is 't read,
That ravenous Kytes of gentle Doves are bred.
Let us first study goodness; then provide,
That from the milk we may their youth so guide,
By holy precepts and good admonitions,
That we may rectify their bad conditions:
It Is of great consequence, what is infused
Into a Vessel when it first is used.
A^de to the rest, in our domesticke state,
^xamples, such as they may imitate.

Mar. It Is hard you speak.

Pam. No wonder, because fair;
^d that is some reason why so hard you are,
^t the more difficult it seems to be,
will ask from us the greater industry.

Mar. Me of a pliant metal you shall find;
^^ then you cast and shape me to your mind.

Pam. Pronounce three words in the interim.

Mar. 'Twere small pain;
^^ words once past, fly never back again.
^^ give you counsel, and consider of it,
which may no doubt redownd to both our profit.
^ic to you our parents to this match,
^^ey once agreed, we would make quick dispatch.

Pam. You would have me, the bush to beat about,
^en in three words you may resolve this doubt.

Mar. Whether I can, is yet to me unknown,
cause I am my parents, not mine own:
other did Contracts in times past proceed,
^esse by the Elders they were first agreed.
^ howsoever, I presume, 'twixt us
is match will prove the more auspitious,
^e casual too, to both, and much more sweet.
^y our parents free consents we meet.
^ move them in it, your office it is, you know,
^use in me it comely would not show:
^ginitie loves to be forced; maids still,
^t they give freely, grant against their will.
Before I move them, shall I thus indent;
I presume I have your free consent?
Thou have, my Pamphilus, then be of cheer.
You Are now to me religiously dear.
But your own voice I Would wish you still suspend,
e're begin, consider first the end.
Do not Affection unto Counsel call,
But summon Reason, which should govern all:
For what Affection swayes is apt to vary,
And is (indeed) no more than temporary:
But that which Reason dictates, be thou sure,
Is permanent, and ever shall endure.

Pam. How sweetly play you the Philosopher?
And I shall no way from your counsels err.

Mar. It shall not much repent you. But again,
There is one doubt that much distracts my brain.

Pam. Now let all scruples vanish.

Mar. Is It your will
I marry to a dead man?

Pam. I live still,
Revived by you.

Mar. The scruple is removed;
And now at length, farewell my best Beloved.

Pam. Be that your care.

Mar. I wish you a glad night.
Whence came that deep suspire?

Pam. From no affright.
A glad night did you say? Now as I live,
What you last wished, would you had will to give.

Mar. It is not fit that too much haste be made,
For yet you see your harvest's in the blade.

Pam. Shall I bear nothing from you?

Mar. This sweet-Ball,
Take it to cheer your heart.

Pam. A kiss withal.

Mar. By no means, since to bring thee, I desire,
A chastity unblemished and entire.

Pam. Can that detract from modesty?

Mar. Desist:
Or would you I by others should be kissed?

Pam. Reserve them then, as these you solely owe
To me and to my use.

Mar. I Will keep them so:
^et I could tell you of another cause
Wherefore I dare not kiss.

Pam. Speake't without pause.

Mar. You say, your whole soul, or the greater part
^ fled into my body; and your heart
^mpty'd of vital heat, (or little there
^emaining still) it therefore is my fear,
^est by a kiss, the little which is left,
^drawing, you be quite of life bereft.
^ut take this hand, symbol of that affection
which mutually confirms our free election.
^o once again farewell: be for my sake
^arefull (I entreat) in that you undertake.
^ean time I Will pray, what yet remains undone,
^ay in a fair and prosperous course be run.

The Argument of the Dialogue betwixt EARTH and AGE.
IN EARTH and AGE is to the life expressed,
How bad all Men are, when they are at best:
How frail, how fading, and in their greet'st glory
Unsettled, wretched, vain, and transitory.
It shows all Learning, Beauty, Youth, and Strength,
All Pomp, all Wealth to nothing comes at length:
No Statue, Structure. Trophee, so sublime,
Which is not quite lost and defaced by Time.
O who can then our common EAR^
I arent blame,
Since all things she produces that have name,
As they have birth from her still-teeming womb,
So the same place is likewise made their tomb.
No wonder then her grief so far exceeds,
Since she is forced to bury all she breeds.
The DIALOGUE.
Earth. What Is he so many tongues can m^ low,
As he had eyes who watch:
(a) Pharian Cow?
So many mouths to me who is he can give,
As Fame reports the (b) Sybels years did live?
^ad I as many words my thoughts to express,
^s (by the (c) Ascraean Poet) we may guess,
^he ancient gods lived days? Had I beside,
^s many brazen throats open and wide,
^s Xerxes shot darts, (after fight begun)
Whose number from the earth shadowed the Sun?
^o many rivulets of tears what is he
^^an to mine eyes infuse, as was by thee
^^rus (if we may trust antiquity)
^et into Ganges drops, thereby to breed
^ry waste unto that (d) Channel drowned his steed?
Who can my clamorous words supply with sorrow?
^o many deep suspires where shall I borrow;
^s Valiant Roman Spirits (scorning to yield)
^ell in one fatal day at (e) Canna's field?
O my great grief, which in the height appears,
Not to be calmed with words, nor washed with tears.
When (f) Phaeton fell from the Suns bright throne,
^ow did his mournful sisters him bemoane?
Who from their rough rindes where they be enclosed,
Weep precious Amber still. Phoebus, opposed
Against (g) Niobe, (her children having slain)
O how she still in marble does complain?
What sorrow, musical Orpheus, did thou feel,
When thy Euridice, stung in the heel,
and dying, borne unto the infernal shade,
Thou with thy harp through hell free passage made?
What more than madness did corrode thy breast,
Andromache? when (Hector laid to rest)
^hou saw thy (i) son, the hope of Troy and thee,
Dropped from a tower: what sorrow might this be?
^^'n such was thine, (k) Aegaeus, to behold
Thy sons black sails returning: which so cold
crook to thy heart, thou thinking Theseus slain,
^eapt from a rock, and gav'st the sea thy name.
The torment of a mighty passion thou
(l) Iocasta felt, to see thy two sons vow
Their mutual ruins by revengefull Arms?
Sad (m) Daedalus, what pitiful alarmes
Were in thy breast given, to behold from hye,
Thy son with his feint wings drop from the sky?
There to be food for fishes, and to add
A name unto that sea, it never had?
Or should I speak how much (n) Progne lamented
Her husbands spowse-breach? or how discontented
(o) Anthonoe was after Actaeon torn?
Or of (p) Antigone, sad and forlorn,
Leading blind Oedipus over rocks along?
Within the compass of my passionate song
Bring all the torments of the former age,
Gyves, Manacles, and Fetters, all that Rage
Or Fury can inflict; want, hunger, thirst,
Whip, post, or prison, labor, or what is worst,
The melancholy dungeon, gallows, rack,
The forke or stake, what on the homicides back
Law can impose, the Traitor or the Thief;
All these are toys, if rated at my grief.
By stings of Serpents, or their teeth, to die;
Rough winter gusts, where Boreas blows most hye:
A thousand wounds were nothing to endure,
Or mounted on a gybbet, there chained sure,
And live to gorge the Ravens, or to bleed
Beneath the Lions jaws; after to feed
Her whe^ps, were nothing.

Age. Of the gods high strain.
What, or whence are you, that so loud exclaim?

Earth. EARTH, Parent of all things.

Age. Why weep you?

Earth. Why?
Have I not just cause? (who so great as I?
Being a Mother) in this wretched state,
To see my Sons hourly snatched hence by Fate,

Age. You have just cause to do it.

Earth. I pray what less
^rceiue you in the untamed Lioness,
^hen she but one whelp misseth from her den?

Age. She mournes.

Earth. What of the ravenous Tygre then,
^ lose her young she tendered with such care?

Age. She grieves and raves.

Earth. How does the poor Hen fare,
^ocking amidst her brood, when in her sight
^he Chicken is snatched from her by the Kite?

Age. She sorrows.

Earth. What does the fleece-bearing Dam,
^hen before her face the Wolf deuours her Lamb?

Age. Laments.

Earth. Does not the Cow with bellowing tear
^he air, to find her Calfe spoyld by the Bear?

Age. Alas she lowes.

Earth. What does the Sow, to spy
^ut any of her Pigs stolen from her stie?

Age. She calls loud after.

Earth. O then what should I?
whatsoever I produce or cherish,
^^ocreate or bear, I see before me perish?
^t not wondrous, Forests should at length
^e putrefaction, rot, and lose their strength?
^e shadowie tree Time of her beauty 'reaves,
spoiling her both of her fruit and leaves.

Age. It Is wondrous I confess, but so 't must be.

Earth. What is it then, that I behold and see
^e brazen statues of the gods decay,
^e monuments of Princes turn to clay;
^ghty (q) Colossi, Temples decked with Veins,
^^ported with rich Columns (by the brains
the best Architects) made wide and large,
^th spacious arches, sacred, in the charge
Of many a golden Relic: these to fall,
And in a few short seasons perish all.

Age. So it has pleased the gods.

Earth. The gods are then
Too cruel and austere to us and men;
Since whatsoever the Earths fertile womb
Brings forth to air, and in the world to have room;
Whatever in her bosom she has taken
To feed and foster: what does now remain,
Or shall hereafter be? That all these must
Needs be involved in rottenness and dust.

Age. It Is fit.

Earth. O anguish never to abate,
Or have cessation!

Age. So the gods will have it.

Earth. Then, as I said before, they are too severe,
And mercilesly in this kind austere.
Is It not enough strong walls are beaten down,
And lofty turrets levelled with the ground;
Cities are sacked, to ruin made a pray,
The famous statues of the gods decay;
That rust the iron does consume and waste,
And pleasant Orchards of corruption taste;
But Man must perish too, and cannot shun
Times fearful havocke, but to ruin run?

Age. The Fates so will.

Earth. What pity can there be
Ascribed to any powerful deity?
But what art thou? What goddess? or how styled?

Age. AGE I am called.

Earth. Hence false Virage, vyld
Infernal Fury; for it is thou alone
Bringst all my Issue to confusion:
Swift feather-footed TIME and ravenous AGE
Devour all things in their remorselesse rage.

Age. What Is sublunarie, Fate will have to fall.

Earth. Say Tyranness, thou AGE, consuming all,
^here be those high Pyramides so famed,
^ which the barbarous (r) Memphis first was named,
^^ar'd by so many workmens sweat and toil?

Age. As all things else, even these have suffered spoil.

Earth. Where Is Pharos Isle? the Sepulcher renownd
^f King (s) Mausolus? where is the Image crowned
^f chaste (t) Diana? Strumpet tell me.

Age. Gone.

Earth. Where Is the (u) Tarpeian Mass, a structure none
^ore famous? where is the hundred gated Town
^alld Thebes? or strong immured Babylon?
Where Is populous Ninive? what is Romes sublime
^ast Theater by Caesar built? by TIME
^onfounded all; where is the Colosse of Rhods?

Age. Their ruins all were foreseen by the gods.

Earth. What Is Troy? old Sparta? or Corinthus hye?
What Is Solomon's Temple, Harlot?

Age. All these lie
^n dark oblivion buried; and in vain
You fret, chide, wrangle, and perplex your brain,
Dear Mother EARTH; weep rivers from thine eyes,
With clamors cleave thy jaws, make thy lungs rise
Consume thy marrow, break thy back, and tear
Thy intrals out; the Fates are so severe,
Thou can not break their order, their strict laws
^nviolate are, and will admit no clause:
^or them the mightiest Kings cannot oppose,
^he Soldiers shield has no defense against those;
The rich man's purse, the learning of the Wise,
No nor the Poets Verse (let that suffice.)

Earth. If then with such ferocitie they be
^o deeply incenst; and that the gods agree
^n such inclemencie: advise me how
^ shall demean me?

Age. You of force must bow
To their eternal doom, though you complain,
Grieve, sorrow, and lament, all is but vain.

Earth. I will not therefore.

Age. Your best is to advise
Man to leave the earth, and look up to the skies:
To put no confidence in Mundane Glory,
Which (like himself) is merely transitory.
Not to grow proud of Beauty, Wisdom, Wealth,
Nor of his Strength, since Age by silent stealth
Will rifle him of all. To him relate,
Of far famed men the most unhappy state.

Earth. Your consolable words have given relief
To my suspense, and now exiled all grief.

Age. That Is all.

Earth. I will obey. Man, answer me.

Man. Who Is that?

Earth. Thy Mother.

Man. Mine? It cannot be.

Earth. Thy mother Earth.

Man. Dear mother then All hail;
What seek you?

Earth. I lament.

Man. Can tears prevail?
Dear Parent cease to grieve: lies it in me
To give least ease to your calamity?

Earth. No, Son.

Man. Why mourn you?

Earth. Have not all things birth
From me thy wretched and sad mother Earth?

Man. I know it well.

Earth. Do thou not see how I
Give to the woods production as they lie?
Sap to the Trees, Increase unto the Grain;
Hug in my fertile bosom stones? Again,
Afford the Vine Grapes, and the tough Oak Mast;
Food to the Fish, and to the Birds repast:
It Is I that to the embroidered meadows yield
^y, to the Gardens Flowers, Grass to the Field:
^d last, as to the best of all my brood,
^th unto Man; and after bearing food.

Man. I do confess it, Mother.

Earth. I much lament,
^are Child, and from hence grows my discontent,
^at having such a fertile womb, so free,
^d ever-teeming; only that by me
^^ many shapes and bodies hourly grow,
^^^firme in substance, and so fair in show,
that nothing can her ravenous throat assuage,
^t all must die and be consumed by Age:
^e ruins Forests, the hard marble wears,
^ets iron, wastes Palaces, strong bulwarks tears,
^ciles Camps, does Citadels demolish quite;
^en the gods sacred statues takes from sight.
^e not high consecrated Temples spares,
^t that which tears and torments to my cares
^ll adds, That Man she ruthlesly deuoures,
and makes him perish at uncertain hours:
therefore beware, my sweetest Child, take heed,
^st tympanous pride within thy bosom breed,
^f this beware, my son.

Man. Mother I shall.

Earth. Then first, lest warlike glory thee assail,
and make thee to forget thou art but Dust;
^eare unto what the god-like Heroes trust,
Whom Age has worn out of all memory.

^ector. Lest any in his potencie rely,
^r in his military arms take pride,
^r powerful skill in (w) Geticke weapons tryde,
^et him consider me, puissant indeed,
^ector the strongest of all Priams Seed,
^otent in battle, and whilst I did stand,
^um was safe, secured by sea and land:
(In borrowed arms) 'twas I Patroclus slew;
Before me, Legions of the Grecians flew,
When I came armed in fury: Troy oppressed
With ten years siege, I guarded with this breast,
I whom alone Achilles quak't to see,
Have yielded unto Fate, and unto thee
Andromache (a widow) left my son.
Thus AGE ends all things an the earth begun.

Achilles. The Trojans terror, Great Achilles, I
In sinewie strength excelling, and thereby
Famous of old, the only hope and stay
Of the Greek Heroes, who alone made way
Through all the Dardan host. 'Twas I alone
Was dreaded in the field, and but me none.
Alone of far-fam'd Hector was I feared,
And Priam quak't when he my name but heard:
Able my nerves, and matchless might my grace,
In body mighty, terrible my face,
Big shoulderd and broad brested, stern my brow;
Yet to (x) Minerva's Altar as I bow,
Paris behind me steals, and with his dart
Wounds me in the heel, which rankles to my heart.
And thus the Valiant perish, and thus AGE
All things consumes in her devouring rage.

Alexander. What Is life but frailty, bubble, or a blast,
A cloud, a smoke, no sooner-seene than past?
Years, like a ball, are voluble, and run;
Hours, like false Vows, no sooner spoke than done:
Time quickly wastes by unwary days,
Nothing can bribe the Sisters to delays.
The horrid sword of Death whosoever would fly,
Let him but look into mine age, how I
Am gone and spent; I that was called and known
By name of Alexander Macedon:
Whose fame has from the Suns vprise been heard
Beyond the place Jove's Son his pillars reard.
Through Hespery and all the Eastern lands
^ave I been famed, whom none (opposed) withstands.
^he populous city Thebes my arm o'rethrew,
many thousand Persian soldiers slew;
^hoenicians, Ciclicks, Paphlagonians, all
^y sword subdued: thrice did Darius fall
^^ neath my potencie: great Babylon,
^ighty in walls, I sieg'd, and seized on.
and after, golden-wav'd Hidaspes past;
^orus (four cubits high) I queld at last,
Whom, conquered, I set free. This done, I then
from India saild, to Babylon again.
^eturning, I fell sick, soon after did;
Thus Time and ravenous AGE shall all things hide,

Sampson. Let Fame, the admirer of all Ancestrie,
And such as are renowned for Chivalrie,
Here show her self, and in her shape divine;
^urveigh all places where the Sun does shine,
^n which large progress let her see the head
Of flowing Nile: or say that she be fled
Unto the Sunburned (y) Garamanti, there
To enquire news, or what she else can hear
From the Numidians or remoat estates
Of (the oft-shifting place) the (z) Sanzonats.
Search Thetis Empire through, or further go
To what the fabric of the world can show,
She shall not find that mortal wight that dare
With me in nerves or strength of arms compare,
I am the mighty Sampson, famous yet,
To whom for strength Alcides would submit:
To strangle Lions was no more than play,
Or to outrun swift Tygres on the way.
What though I with the jaw bone of an ass
A thousand slew, and through their army pass?
What though the city gates I rend and tear,
And (after) them upon my shoulders bear?
Yet notwithstanding my great power and strength,
I yield to death, Age swallows all at length.

Earth. Know now my Son, that such most happy are,
Whom others harms can teach how to beware.
See, whatsoever I produce or bring,
Nurse or give fostring to, even every thing
Devouring Age consumes. Do thou not see
Renowned Hector yield to Destiny?
How great Achilles, after wars rough storms,
Despoiled of life, to be the food for worms?
Sampson and Alexander in their prime,
Though strong, yet they both perished: This can Time.
Now lest fair Feature should in thee breed pride,
Natures indowments, or ought else beside;
See women next, in face and form excelling,
Swallowed in dust; all Beauty Age expelling.

Hellen. O you blind men, with feminine shape oretaken
Whose amorous hearts are with their culture shaken,
Now do I find too late, and grieve to think,
All mortal beauty must in Lethe sink.
We kembe these hairs, and trim them up in gold.
(Our curled tresses with rich gems inrol'd)
Our fronts we burnish, and there cannot pass
One blemish, but corrected by the glass.
By art we adorn our heads, and by art we
Dispose the face and hair; by art we see.
And yet these hairs, this head, these eyes, this face,
Vanish like moving waves which flote apace.
Behold! I that was fair, am worms meat made,
My flesh corrupt, and buried in the shade.
Behold (I say) that Grecian Hellen, she
Raped, Menelaus, in her prime from thee:
Me (a) Theseus ravished first, and left me so,
That saving kisses I did nothing know.
False Paris last (by Fate or Fury led)
Hosting with me, made stealth into my bed:
Fool that he was, he little then did know,
^his snare for me was Troys sad overthrow.
This putrified Coarse by him so bought,
was after by a thousand ships re-sought.
O Greece, what preparation did thou make,
To fetch that flesh which now the worms forsake?
What broiles? what strage? what slaughter to destroy,
Did this loathed carcass breed 'twixt Greece and Troy?
Became it thee, friend Paris, to forsake
Thy household gods, and such a journey take,
To hazard seas, only to fetch away
from Greece this rottenness, this putrid Clay?
and you the (b) Atrides, would you sail so far,
and for this dust maintain a ten years war?
that this vile earth, this stench you might return,
To close these ashes in my fathers urne?
^ais. If any fables have bin sung in praise
Of Prostitutes, what fame their shapes could raise;
^ the Corinthian Lais, choice and best,
^aue been the crown and grace to all the rest.
^y chin the Ivorie stained, Lillies my brow,
^o match mine eyes the world then knew not how:
^y neck was long and straight, and my veins blew,
often lips, in my clear cheeks fresh roses grew;
^y nose was neither crooked, long, nor flat,
^y visage it became, it graced that:
^y wanton paps like two round hillocks grow,
^^om which moist springs two milky rivers flow.
^y belly comely sweld, for it became
^ike a plumpe Peacocks, soft as the young lamb:
^y stomach like the temperat Turtles feeding;
^odest my diet, and no surfets breeding;
^y arms much whiter than the Lillies shwoing,
^r flowers, (d) Alcinous, in thy garden growing.
^ho that my leg did look on, but did think
^e burnt in flames, or in the seas did sink?
Or who my back parts did behold, but sed,
O that I were a flea in Lais bed.
Or who my foot, but wished himself a stone,
With upward eyes for me to tread upon.
And yet this face, these cheeks, these lips, these eyes,
This neck, these hairs, these temples, legs and thighs,
This stomach, belly, back, arms, hands, and feet
Are worms meat now, and with corruption meet.
Learn young man then, that which we trust in most
Is dust and filth; in Age are all things lost.
Thisbe. The Babylonian Thisbe is my name,
Noble my birth, my beauty great in fame;
No lovely Maid that had in the Orient place,
But with much envy gazed me in the face.
Enraged Jove I with a smile could please,
Or pull his threatening thunder back with ease.
Juno her self of me has jealous bin,
And feared lest Jove in Babylon would sin.
The white (d) Caistrian Bird to me did yield,
And to my blush the Roses of the field.
Yet not this feature, not this front or face,
Nor these mine eyes, to which the stars gave place,
Could ransom me from the worms fearful rage,
Or the rude phangs of all-devouring Age.

Lucretia. Who the divining Sybels shall commend,
Or thee, (e) Penelope, and not offend?
Of (f) Dido's feature who shall smoothly write?
Or the (g) Leucadian sisters beauty cite?
Behold me Lucrece, softer than the down,
Or the swans breast, and whiter: who was known
More tractable than wax; fresh as the air,
Softer my skin than the ripe Melons are.
With this fair body I the worms have fed,
And a small urne contains me being dead.
These paps, that (h) Cato the Severe would turn,
Or chaste (i) Hippolitus in ardor burn.
This precious flesh, this shape is changed to dust
and putrefaction, to which all may trust.
(...) him, the earth brings forth, but Age can wast,
(...) and the same fate meets with all at last.

Earth. Consider then, my Son, these shapes you have,
(...) endor nor feature, ransoms from the Grave:
That all things suffer change, neck, breast, and throat,
^ips, cheeks, brow, stomach, all on which we dote,
^onvert to ashes. Yet lest thou be won,
Thinking to escape by other gifts; my son
^trend with prepared ears, hear what the Learnd,
^he Rich and others have 'tofore discernd;
These and the rest have the same accent sung:
^ow whilst they speak, thou still suppress thy tongue.

Virgil. If Learning from himself shall man divide,
and make him like the Peacocke strut with pride,
^e offends in madness, sencelesly is vain.
^e hold, I Virgil, of the learned strain,
^f Poets Prince, their glory and their grace,
^o whom Apollo did afford prime place;
^e the most sacred Muses favored still,
^or me the (k) Driades their laps would fill
With various flowers, and the Napaec bring
^haplets of Bays to crown me when I sing.
^o the Palaces of Emperors accited,
and to the banquets of great Kings invited:
and yet I died. What profit did it breed,
that I first taught the wanton Goats to feed,
^o till, to sow and reap; or be sam'd far
^or the rude slaughters of a ten years war?
^et was I food for worms. What Is Poesy then?
^stable Age ends what she will, and when.

Xerxes. Lest opulencie should elate man high,
and make him set his face against the sky,
^^ust to his youth, or what his riches brings,
^ehold me Xerxes, mightiest of all Kings,
And most magnipotent, I that have bin
Possessed of such an infinite Magozin
Of gold and treasure, so immense a store,
As never Persian King enjoyd before;
That when my pride toward Grecia'gan to aspire,
Gave to so many soldiers food and hire;
So many legions from the Orient brought,
That in the first great battle which we fought,
Such store of shafts and darts my camp did yield,
As kept the Suns bright luster from the field:
So many ships of mine the Ocean swayd,
As made astonished Neptune fly, afraid,
And hide him in his Deeps, What Is plenty then?
Or what does Pomp or Greatness profit men?
We vanish all like shadows: and even thus
Died (l) Croesus, (m) Crassus, (n) Midas, (o) Priamus,
(p) Pigmalion, whom both Age and Death constraines
To walk with Xerxes in the Elysian plains.

Nero. If any air to Tyrants breathing gives;
If any (q) Catiline or (r) Marius lives;
Or if there any stern (s) Mezentius be,
Contemner of the gods: these look on me,
I the base sink of sin, the ship of shame,
Quaffer of humane blood, Nero, the same
Whose murders have been bruted over all,
From the Suns uprise, to his Western fall:
Whose gluttonies and lusts Nilus knew plain,
And (t) Calpes, to the farthest parts of Spain.
To rip my mothers womb was my desire:
Who knows not too, I set great Rome on fire?
Who knows not, that my fury did betray
The lives of Lucian and wise Seneca?
Who knows not, that Saint Paul and Peter tryde
My sword, by which most of the Senate died?
But what was then my miserable fate?
Pressed by my fears, and by the peoples hate,
Scornd by each sex, abhorred in mine own land,
Contemned of all, I fell by mine own hand:
Thus Nero died, thus none can AGE withstand.

Sardanapal. Lest soft effeminacie, lust, and abuse
Of Natures gifts, might plead the least excuse;
I am that Sensuallist Sardanapal,
Who to my self thinking to ingrosse all
Voluptuousness, decked in their womanish suits,
I spent my time amongst common Prostitutes;
False periwigs upon my head I wore,
And being man, the shape of woman bore.
Yet this rank body a small urne contains;
To this we must, to this, AGE all constraines.

Earth. Son do thou see how all things Age outweares?
How the Strong perish, with the prime in years?
How the Fair falls, and how the Learned decay?
And how the Rich consume and fade away?
How Tyrants dye? How death the Wanton tastes?
And, to conclude, how swift Time all things wastes?

Man. What (Mother) shall I do? If I live chaste,
I am not therefore safe: or if I wast
My hours in Venus sports, I am not free:
If ever weep, what shall become of me?
If ever sport, what profit can it bring?
And though I ever mourn, or ever sing,
All Is one, for die I must. Since Death ends all,
Let my corrupted body die and fall
To dust, to earth or worms, pleasure's my store,
Let me enjoy that, I desire no more.

Earth. Thus I conclude; Though man's life be vnstayd,
And as we see, by Custom hourly fade,
Even as the parched leaves by Autumn change
And fall to nothing; yet (which is most strange)
Of his own fruit he is unmindful still,
And follows what proves to himself most ill.

The Argument of the Dialogue entitled MISANTHROPOS, or the Man-Hater.
This Dialogue of Riches does entreat;
Of their true use: how they with lucre great
Are long acquired, and how soon lost. The cause
Of this Discourse is grounded from the applause
Timon first had in Athens, where he swayed,
For his wealths sake, being honored and obay'd.
Who after a most riotous expense,
Having consumed his state, and grown to sense
Of Poverty; such as he raised he tries,
But finds them now his person to despise.
He seeing how base avarice did blind
The world that time, in hate of all Mankind,
