So devious from Virtue, did propose
A new name to himself, MISANTHROPOS;
Which gives this Tractat name. The Authors intent
Being to show, how proud and insolent
Riches make men: and have it understood,
How they pursue the Bad, but fly the Good.
Read and observe, this Dialogue affords
Much excellent matter, coucht up in few words.
The DIALOGVE.
Timon. O Jupiter, loving and sociable,
That art domestical and hospitable,
The lightning-blaster, Oath and Iury-shaker,
Cloud-gathering god, and the great Thunder-maker:
Or if thou any other syr-name have,
^uch as by the ancient Poets in times past
Has to thy deity been madly given,
To patch their halting Verse, and make 't run even,
For thee a thousand nicknames are pursuing,
^o help their Lines, and keep their Rymes from ruin)
Where Is now thy all-fear'd lightning, breeding wonder?
Where Is thine high streperous and loud voic'd thunder?
Thy radiant and bright burning bolts (once dreaded)
What, are thy late keen pointed darts unheaded?
^ll these, since thou with-heldst thy terrible stroke,
^ppeare vain trifles, and Poeticke smoke,
and of thy great power nothing else proclaims,
^ave mere verbositie, and noise of Names,
^or these thy Poetised tools for war,
Which being drawn, both reached and wounded far;
^ know not by what means, but now at length,
^unt is their chastning edge, and lost their strength;
^o cold and frozen they about thee lie,
That of thy wrath no spark we can epsy
^^ndled against the Nocent. These perjurers
^esting at sufferance) make themselves assurers
Of their own safety: being no more afraid
^f thy unquenchable lightning, than dismayed
^t common fire extinguished: it shows l^ke
^o them, as if thou should some Tition strike,
and they look on; dreading no more thine ire,
^han his whose struggling breathes forth Aetna's fire:
Presuming no more wound belongs vnto't,
Than only to be smudg'd and grim'd with soot.
From hence it comes, that (a) Salmoneus dare
With thee in thy loud thunders to compare:
Nor strange; he a man that bold and daring is,
And thou a god so sufferant and remiss:
What could he less do than such revels keep,
Since thou have drunk (b) Mandragora, to sleep
And snort away thy time? even still forbearing
Such as blaspheme and never cease forswearing.
Besides, like one that such misdoers tenders,
Not plaguing them, thou plumpst up great offenders.
Some hold thee blind, and cannot see what is done:
Some, easy to be foold: like rumors run,
That thou art deaf on both sides: others hold,
Thou art decrepit, and of late grown old.
When thou wast in thy former youth and prime,
Thou did not sloathfully mis-spend thy time;
Then thou had spleen, and unto wrath wast prone,
Vengeance and just infliction graced thy throne,
And wast indeed such an all-dreaded god,
No male factor could escape thy rod:
Thou heldst with such no covenant, but thy darts
Were still in action to amase their hearts;
Thy invulnerable arm advancing hye,
Whilst through the earth thy flashing lightnings fly,
Drawn from thy quiver, where they late did stick,
Shot as from warring Archers, swift and thick.
Besides these, fearful earthquakes, which were man
such as her reverend breast tare up and cranny
Mountains of snow by drifts made, hail in such
Abundance, that of late we see none such:
Impetuous showers of rain made torrents rise.
And rivers over their banks to tyrannise.
It has been said, In good (c) Deucalions age
Such sudden inundations 'gan to rage,
That all mankind being drowned in one account,
^arse was one skyffe saved on (d) Licoris Mount;
^ that, Humanities small seeds reserving:
^^om whence a generation less deserving,
and much more impious grew: they imitating
^hat's bad, and worse and worse still propagating.
Nor is there cause thou should with them be wroth;
^eceiving but the guerdon of thy sloth.
^ho now unto thy Altars offerings bring?
^r to thy dreadful name loud Poems sing?
^hou now have neither sacrifice nor praise,
^or is thy ruinous Temple hung with Bays;
^nlesse by chance some by Olympus pass,
and call to mind that such a god once was,
And rather too for fashion sake, than fear)
^erhaps some thrifty Offering may leave there:
^ike Saturn they would deal with thee (I tell thee)
and (as thou him) so from thy throne expel thee.
I here omit, whilst thou have elsewhere trifled,
^ow often thy great Temple has been rifled,
^ansackt and spoild, whilst thou the loud tongued Crier
O'regrowne with sloth, as if thou did desire
Thine own undoing) not once wake nor call
The dogs there kenel'd, make them bark and ball,
Nor raise the drowsy neighbors, sleeping fast,
^o present rescue, till the thieves were past:
^ut thou the generous Giant tamer, who
^ost boast in the great Giants overthrow,
Did like a sot sit neither graced nor feared,
Whilst from thy chin they shaved away thy beard:
Yet thou even at that instant were so strong,
^o hold a dart that was ten cubits long.
O thou so famous, what will thou endure
^n the end, if still thou will be thus secure?
Or at what time will thou extirp the seeds
By thy just vengeance) of those gross misdeeds?
How many bold aspiring Phaetons, or
Deucalions can thou find? Hie expiat for
This inexhausted wickedness still flowing
From corrupt mankind, and thou all this knowing
Impertinent things I will submit to Fate,
And pass in silence: only now relate
Mine own particular wrongs. How many great
And mighty of the Athenians, to the seat
Of known sublimitie has Timon raised,
Creating them from beggars? whilst they praised
And magnified my bounty. Unto all
I spread my open hand and liberal;
In which most men (before me) I exceeded,
As generally supplying such as needed,
My riches' among my friends parced and given,
Till I my self to penury was driven.
Then suddenly a stranger I was grown,
And to my most familiar friends not known:
Those (when I past them) that would croutch and ben^
In adoration: those that did depend
Upon my grace, my presence cannot brook,
Nor on my wants so much as daigne a look.
If (as sometimes) I chance to cross the street,
And any one of these my Creatures meet.
"As of some statue, by long time decaid,
"They shun my shadow, of my fall afraid.
And others likewise that from far espy me,
Into some by-lane skrew themselves, so fly me,
Make me an ominous spectacle of Fate,
As if malevolent and unfortunate:
Who in my better days was their Director,
Styled by themselves, their Father and Protector.
These mischiefs growing, to be made so vile,
My own deep counsels I'gan reconcile,
Snatched up this mattocke, chus'd a field out, where
The Earths fair breast I am forced to wound and ear^
And thus my time in labor wear away,
^^^ng hyr'd for some four half pence by the day.
^us with my spade in solitude here I
^de to my self mine own Philosophy.
^e profit reapt hence is, to be remoat,
^^d live out of the sight of such as dote
^ smoky vanities, those that inherit
^nty of all things, and yet nothing merit;
^^d that does most torment me. Now at length,
Jupiter
^aturn and Rheas offspring show thy strength;
^y profound sleep shake off, for thou indeed
sloth do (e) Epimenides exceed.
and once again thy Trisulk, and retire
^ Oeta, and there kindle 't with new fire:
^ng full of flames, when they most hotly glow,
^rt of that vengefull indignation show
which to thine high Tribunal did belong,
^hen thou were Jupiter the young and strong:
^e still to those reproaches subject be,
^e Cretans cast upon thy Tomb and thee.

Jupiter. What is he, so vociferously exclaimes,
Mercury, and Us so often names?
^s tedious clamors in mine ears sound shrill
^eere unto Athens) from Himettus hill,
^^ at the mountains foot, deject and sad,
^e, meager, lame, and in a goats skin clad?
seems to me that delving is his trade,
^s eyes cast down, he leanes upon his spade:
is a bold speaking fellow, confident too
what he says. After this sort to do
^losophers were wont, and they alone,
^d it is a wonder but this fellow's one,
^^at dares against our deity devise
^h impious and unheard of blasphemies.

Mercury. Do you not know him (Father) thus forlorn,
^n to Echicratides, in Collite borne;

Timon his name, with whom we both have guested,
And in our annual Sacreds often feasted:
He on the sudden with such plenty filled,
Who at the altars of the gods has killed
Whole Hecatombs, and in his height of wealth
Has quaft unto us many a grateful health.

Jupiter. Whence comes this sudden change? But is this
The honest rich man that was known so free,
Whom Athens with her loud encomiums graced,
And such a multitude of friends embraced?
How happens it he is so poorly arrayd,
So miserably dejected and dismayed?
I guess him by the spade on which he leanes,
Some painful labourer that works for means.

Merc. You see how his humanity has changed him
And freeness, from his dearest friends estranged him:
His mercy unto others, being so kind,
And then amongst so many not to find
One grateful, has distraction in him bred,
Still to be living, but to them thought dead.
Considering next how he is scorned, derided,
And his revenue and estate divided,
Not amongst Crows and Wolves, but worser far,
Ravenous and tearing vultures, who still ar^
Gnawing upon his liver; those whom he
His friends and best familiars thought to be.
For they who now in his abundance swim,
Were more delighted in his feasts than him:
Nay, those who at his table did applaud him;
When even unto the bare bones they had gnawne him
They sucked his very marrow, and then fled;
So to the world gave him both lost and dead:
Being so far, from misery to free him,
They would not seem to know him when they see ^^
These brought him to this base despised trade,
And hurld him from the Scepter to the Spade;
^^'d him out of his purple, here to sweat
^ hardly earne his meat before he eat:
which he is so possessed with mortal spleen
^nst mankind that so ingrate has been;
^e whom his bounty raised and brought to fame,
^se now remember Timon had a name.

(...). Yet one (believe me) not to be rejected,
^ for his former piety respected.
^ blame I him his anger to be such,
^en ungrateful to endure so much.
^s zealous and good man not to redeem,
favor his afflictions we might seem:
we much pity him, who to maintain
^ adoration, has before us slain
many Goats and Bulls, and those the best
at his flocks yielded; so that I protest,
^^d approve them for my service meet,
^ose savor in my nosthrils still smells sweet.
^s for the boldness of that infinite Crew
^ase perjurers, who forsware what is true;
^ikewise those in self-conceit so strong,
^y make no conscience of what is right or wrong;
^^ as insult by rapine and rude force,
pressing without mercy or remorse,
Sacrilegious too, such as forbear
^^eir public robberies, not through love but fear;
many the are in number, (though I strive)
their misdeeds I no way can connive.
^t mine eye of late on Athens, where
many strange Duels and fencings were,
^ Pro's and Contra's, quarrels in the schools,
^e mad men railing, some; others like fools
^ing: in uproar all, shrill acclamations
^colding Disputants; such vociferations,
I those so loudly thundered in mine ear,
^^ suppliants plaints I could by no means hear.
Therefore with stopped ears I must silent sit,
Or with their confused noise be tortured yet.
There Is a new toy imagined by these Nodies,
Of things essential, and yet wanting bodies;
Mere fantasies, which they with might and maine
(Though nothing) to have being would maintain:
Which is the cause I have been so unkind,
As this well meaning man not once to mind.
It now remains his goodness to requite:
Hye therefore Mercury, Plutus accite,
With all speed possible command him hither,
And bring with you a magozin together
Of new coined gold, more than the man can tell.
He with his treasure shall with Timon dwell.
Nor shall they easily be removed from thence,
Though by his bounty and too large expense,
He would expel them from him. For those Chattere^
Parrots and Pyes, with other oily flatterers
And Parasits that have ungrateful bin,
I now will study to chastise their sin,
So soon as I my vengefull darts have viewd,
And my three-forked thunder stone renewed:
Some of the rays are broke, others rebated,
Which with all speed I must have inst^urated:
The points are dulled, since I insenced was
Against the Sophist Anaxagoras,
Who to his Scholars openly professed,
The gods or were not, or were naught at least:
But I through error mist, Pe^icles bestrid him,
And with his body from my vengeance hid him.
The bolt averted light upon the phane
Where the two brothers deify'de remain,
(Castor and Pollux) burnt it to the ground.
And not one stone was left about it sound.
But what a punishment will this appear
Unto those envious wretches, when they hear,
Timon, in whose oppression they agreed,
^all them in wealth and potencie exceed;

Mercury. O but much more availes it for a man
^o stretch his throat with all the power he can,
^o be obstreperous and heard from far;
^o not mean the balling at the Bar,
^ud railing for fat fees and gain of gold;
^t those like Timon, clamorous and bold,
^ho in his Orisons has been so shrill,
^ make great Jove attentive against his will;
^ho had he (smothering grief) sat still and mute,
^^ght have long labored in a threadbare su^e.

Plutus. To him, o Jupiter, I will not go.

Jupiter. Tell me, o excellent Plutus, wherefore so?
^pecially when thou by us art sent.

Plutus. Because I have a fearful president:
^ he with many injuries afflicted,
^hen I was wholly to his love adicted,
^e shook me off, as one that did deride me,
and into mamocks and small bits divide me,
^en cut me into pieces: would not sell me,
^t being his domesticke friend expel me
^ith forks and prongs, as one insenc'd with ire,
^ casting from his hand hot coals of fire.
and shall I once again enter his doors,
^ be consumed on Sycophants and whores,
^^atterers and such? Send me, o Jove, I entreat,
^o some that understand a gift so great,
^^m that to incorporat and hug me strives,
^ such as prize me dearer than their lives.
^his stupid fellow has a covenant made
^ith Poverty, preferring a poor trade:
mattocke and a skin-coat from her took,
fore my golden and all-tempting look:
no now with four small halfe-pence can make shift,
^d yet has given ten talents at a gift.

Jupiter. But Timon no such thing hereafter dares
Against thy person: rather he prepares
To honor thee, as one whom Toil and pain
Has reconciled, to welcome thee again;
His intrals with long fast and hunger clung,
Has with his mind now likewise changed his tongue
But thou art too complaintive, who accuses
First Timon to me for his late abuses,
Because he with his gates set open wide,
Gave thee free-leaue, there or elsewhere to abide;
Not keeping thee in obscure prison fast,
(As being jealous of thee) where thou have
Thy liberty. Again, thou art enraged
Against those Cormorants that have incag'd
And shut thee up; complaining, Beneath locks,
Keys, bolts, and seals thou art kept as in the stocks,
From whence thou can not move, from light exclud^
Living in dungeons and dark holes contruded:
Of such thou have complaind to me, and wept,
To be so long, so close in darkness kept;
Looking withal so meager, pale, and wan,
Oppressed with care as had thou been a man,
Starved and shrunk up, thy sinues drawn together,
Thy fingers clutcht and lamed; I know not whether
Hoording up gold this Apoplex compelling,
Or numnesse, made by thy assiduat telling;
Willing to stay with them by no persuasion,
But apt to leave them on the least occasion.
And what above thought makes thee ill bested,
Is, in an iron or a brazen bed
(As thou have heard of Danae) to be laid,
As there for ever to be kept a maid,
By impious overseers schoold and taught,
Who save in gain and usurie know naught.
Their gross absurdities I have heard thee note,
Who on thy person above reason dote;
And being in their power, dare not employ them,
Or lying prostrat to their lust, enjoy them:
They all the while strict vigilancie keeping,
With guard upon the place where thou art sleeping,
^ying the bolts and bars, and winking never,
^s in great hope thou will supply them ever,
and have much profit from thee. Not that they
^ean to make blessed use of thee though they may,
^ut only keep thee in such strict tuition,
because none else of thee should have fruition.
^st like a dog that in the manger lies,
^ho though himself the provender despise,
^s to his pallat a distastefull meat,
^et will not suffer the poor horse to eat.
I likewise have observed thee laugh at those,
Who though they have thee at their free dispose,
^ost gripple are in sparing. In a word,
^hou holdst it most radiculous and absurd,
that such (mean time) should starve themselves, not knowing
^o whom (their flower being withered) thou art growing:
^o what Executor, Servant, or Page,
^eward or Pedagogue, who their spent age
^aue not bestowed on thee, but on thy come,
^o seize by force, or else by stealth purloine;
and then for his safe hoording and close hiding,
^he wretched Master (new deceast) deriding,
^ho did so charily in his life time lock it,
and with a snuff half burnt within the sockit,
^r dry rush light, keep wakefull his faint eyes
upon his (now) all-forfeit vsuries.
^^t not therefore, Plutus, ill in thee,
that have of these so often complained to me;
^hy fickle thoughts so suddenly to vary,
and blame in Timon the clean contrary?

Plutus. Yet if my cause to censure be referred,
^e shall confess that I have no way erred:
Nor is there reason why I should dispense
With Timons lightness, rather negligence,
In stead of study, care, and that good will,
Respect, and love, that should attend me still.
Nor of the adverse part do I approve,
Those that embrace me with an over-love,
Imprisoning and obtruding me so close,
To make me every day more huge and gross;
Franking me up, to fat me, with intent
I may appear to them more corpulent;
Yet they themselves, nor use me in my neatness,
Nor show me unto others in my greatness.
All such I contumelious hold and mad,
Who notwithstanding all good from me had,
Put me in shackles, where I starving ly,
Oppressed with hunger, and with thirst still dry:
Not understanding they must shortly leave me
To such as stand wide gaping to receive me.
Nor do I of those Prodigass allow,
Apt to part with me, and not caring how:
Such only I approve amongst the rest,
Who hold a mediocritie the best;
That neither vow to keep an absolute fast,
Or having plenty, are inclined to wast.
Consider this, o Jove, Say that a man
Find for his choice the fairest Maid he can,
To make his Bride; and when the Nuptial night
Invites them both to rest, he sets her light,
Neither observes her, nor is tender over her,
But sets his doors and gates broad wide before her,
To gad and wander at her pleasure, trusts
Her night and day to prostrate where she lusts:
The man that gives such liberty to vice,
What does he (not preventing) but entice
To lewdness? as inviting folk to prove her:
Can such an one be said truly to love her?
Again, If any shall a Fair one wive,
And bring her to his house; when he should strive
To play the husband, and to procreate
Children as hopeful as legitimate:
Even then of all due Mariage-sweets should grutch her,
Nor in her flourishing prime of beauty touch her;
Unwilling from a loathsome Jail to free her,
Where nor himself nor any else may see her.
But thus secluded, barren, and deprived,
Shall keep her still a virgin, though long lived:
And then, That all this was for love pretend,
Preferring her thus old and near her end,
With an exhausted body, color pale,
Deep wrinkled cheeks, and sunk-in eyes that fail;
Would you not think that man quite from his senses,
Who when by lawful and most just pretenses
He might have hopeful Issue, and possess
A goodly sweet young woman, and no less
Amorous, yet suffers her in care and anguish,
^adly like one of Ceres Priests to languish?
Thus used and I abused, am sometimes torn,
^^fled and plucked in pieces, and in scorn
^affled and kicked: by others kept alive,
^mprison'd like some branded fugitive.

Jupiter. Why fretst thou against those made to endure
^trange punishments for sins black and impure?
Or wherefore art thou at such slaves astonished,
Who in themselves see their own vices punished:
The one like (h) Tantalus, in sight of meat,
And always gaping, but forbid to eat:
With such dry chaps they gape upon their gold,
Hot with that sated which they still behold.
The other, though they have it in their paws,
^eady to glut themselves; from their starved jaws
The Harpies snatch it, as from (g) Phineus, spoiling
those dainties for which he so long was toiling.
Go thou from Us to Timon without fear,
To whom (no doubt) thou will be henceforth dear.

Plutus. But think you that at length he will forbear
To pour me into leaking vessels, where
Though with great labor you maintain it still,
The liquor runs out faster than you fill;
Sooner exhausting me, to draw me dry,
Than I my self can with my self supply:
He fearing when I shall with plenty crown him,
I have but merely laid a plot to drown him.
I shall be as in (i) Danaus daughters tons,
No sooner ought poured in, but out it runs;
So many holes being in the bottom drild,
That it draines faster than it can be filled.

Jupiter. But though the liquor through the vessel breaks,
And that he has no will to stop these leaks,
But by perpetual dropping and effusion,
All must of force be wasted in conclusion:
Yet amongst the lees and dregs no doubt he will find
His leathern pelt and spade still left behind.
Go you mean time and see the man possessed
Of treasure in abundance, and the best.
That done, o Hermes, call at Aetna, where
The (k) Cyclops are at work, and (do thou hear?)
Bid them repair to me at my first sending,
For tell them that my three tynd bolt wants mending.
Both edge and point is dulled and in my spleen
I now must have it sharpened and made keen.

Merc. Plutus let us walk. But stay (thou of such fame)
Tell me how on the sudden came thou lame?
What, and blind too?

Plutus. These imperfections lie
Not always, Hermes, in my foot or eye;
Only at some set times. For being sent
By Jove, I am thus lame incontinent,
I know not by what means compeld vntoo't,
But instantly I halt on either foot,
And ere the place before me reach I can,
I am grown a lame decrepit weak old man.
But if I be to part from such, I fly
Swifter than birds make way beneath the sky;
No bars can stop me, furlongs are no more
To me, than narrow strides, I strip before
The winds swift wings, and can deceive the eye
With my unparaleld velocitie:
Nay even the public Criers have agreed
To crown me Victor for my pace and speed.

Merc. I now perceive thou Plutus idly pratest,
Since all things are not true that thou relatest:
How many have I known but yesterday
Ready to hang themselves, that could not pay
One single halfpenny down upon the nail,
To buy an halter with: yet now they sail
In gold and purple; some in Chariots ride,
That had not late a poor Ass to bestride,
Wealth flowing on them in so swift a stream,
That they themselves have thought it but a dream.

Plutus. A thing quite contrary it is, I vow,
Of which, o Mercury, thou twitst me now:
For know, I walk not on mine own legs when
I am sent by Jove to honest and good men.
But if god (l) Dis shall once command, I run,
For his behest is in an instant don.
He of the great gift-Giuer bears the name,
His Magozin's in hell, whence gold first came:
And therefore when I shift from man to man,
With all the industry and care they can,
They take me, wrapped and swathed in Bonds and Bills,
Where one conveyance a whole sheep-skin fills:
So, signed and sealed, me in some box they smother,
And toss me 'twixt one party and another.
The owner dead, left in some obscure place,
Where Dogs and Cats may piss upon his face.
Those that have hope to enjoy me are soon found
In The Courts, and those hot sented as the hound,
Yawning like to the Swallows infant brood,
When the dam fluttering to their nest brings food.
Now when the seale's discovered on the Will.
And the string cut that bound the roll up, still
They gape to see the parchment op't and read,
To know the Executor to the late Dead.
Then instantly a new heir is proclaimed.
And either, there, some greasy kinsman named,
Some Sycophant or fawning Parasite,
Or else perhaps a debosht Catamite.
He with a new shaved chin, being of this treasure
Possessed, then studies noveltie and pleasure,
With all rarieties at the height rated,
Which the dead hoorder in his life time hated.
He must be then a gentleman at least,
And with his wealth his Title (needs) encreast,
With change of name: for he that was before
Known by the name of (m) Pyrrhias, Drono, or
Tibias; although the man be still the same,
Must either Megabyzus have to name,
Megacles or Protarchus: his mind swelling
With vain ostent to gain a style excelling.
Even those that did not yawne with deep inspection
(Though at the first in like state and election)
Into these hidden Mines; now all dis-jointed,
When they behold each other disappointed,
Although they truly mourn, seen but to feet,
To see the small fish Tuny escape the net;
Who as he living did but little eat,
So being dead could not afford much meat.
Now he that grovelling falls upon this Mass,
(Some fat fed Budget, or dull witted Ass,
Who of no good parts or clean life has bin)
Enters upon it with an unwasht skin:
None treads so softly by him, but he fears,
And like a curre then starts up with pricked ears,
His fellow footmen he despises now,
To the Temple and the Horse-mill does allow
An adoration equal. Who to dispense
^s able now with his great insolence?
^nsufferable he grows, the Good despising,
And over his Like and equals tyrannising;
Vaunting in mighty things, till Lust, incited
With some fair whore, or otherwise delighted
^n keeping Dogs and Horses, or by hearing
His trencher-Flies about his table jearing,
And whispering to him, He is grown more fair
Than the Greek (n) Nereus, Homer made so rare:
The mischiefe's, he believes it; their verbositie
Persuading him, That in true generositie
^ Cecrops and Codrus come behind him. One
Tells him, Ulysses unto him alone
^ubmits in wisdom, and persuades the Beast
To be more rich than Croesus was, at least
^y sixteen fold: exhausting by this mean,
And in one breath of time consuming clean
What was by piecemeale gathered, and did rise
From base extortions, thefts, and perjuries.

Merc. These are no question true: but when thou go
On thine own feet (being blind) say how thou know
The way thou art to take? how can thou find
^uch men as are of good and honest mind?
To whom (as now) my father often times sends thee,
and in his care and providence commends thee.

Plutus. Think thou I find those I am sent unto?

Merc. By Jove not I: if so, how did thou do,
When lately being to Aristides sent,
^hou to Hipponicus and Callius went,
and other base Athenians, scarce worth thought,
Or a poor single halfpenny, to be bought?
What is the course thou take upon the way?

Plutus. Now high, now low, in each blind path I stray,
Till unawares upon some one I fall,
And be he what he will, that man gets all:
He that is next me and can first catch hold,
To fasten on me, having seized my gold,
Secludes me to some obscure place, possessing
What he long wished, then openly confessing,
In prayers and vows, he is to Hermes bound,
By whose assistance this great fortune's found.

Merc. Is Jove deceived, presuming that thou go
To inr^ch such as he affects most,
And thinks them worthy of his largess?

Plutus. Right,
O Mercury, and justly too, my sight
Being defective, and at such times blind;
And sending me to seek that, which to find
So difficult is, and scarcely has a Being,
Is that a task with my dim sight agreeing?
In which had quick eyd Argus in my sted
been his inquisitor, he scarce had sped:
The path so narrow and obscure, beside,
It being so rare to see a good man guide
A Cities weal; for those corrupt still sway,
And those in numbers flocking in my way:
I groping, can I possibly eschew
To avoid the many, and select the few?
The wicked always yawning after gains,
(The others not) how can I escape their trains?

Merc. I but how comes it, when thou art to forsake
These wretches, thou such voluble speed do make?
And without rub or the least stumbling, when
Thou can not see the path before thee?

Plutus. Then
Both eyes and feet assist, and then alone,
When Time invites and calls me to be gone.

Merc. Another thing resolve me: Tell me how
^comes to pass (o god of Wealth) that thou
^rst being blind, next, of a pale complexion,

^f so many great friends and lovers, such

^^fore of thee undoubtedly possessed?
^gaine, If he that after thee enquires,
^ance to be frustrat in his hot desires;
^or such I have known many, and some noted,

that at their courting if thou seem'dst but coy,
^ave ready been their own lives to destroy:
Who when they saw they Plutus could not please,
themselves from hye rocks cast into the seas.
and yet I know, and thou must needs confess,
View but thy self as I do) thou will guess,
^ not conclude, it is not love, but madness
^akes them despair in doting on thy badnesse.

^ the same form as thou beholdst me here,
Or lame or blind, with such defects about me?

Merc. O by no means, for I should then misdoubt me
that they were blind as thou art.

Plutus. But not quite,
^ Mercury, like me deprived of sight:
and yet there falls on them, as by some chance,
^ kind of error or blind ignorance,
Which occupies them all, over their eyes
^asting a shadowie filme, which does disguise
^y deformed parts; so I appear to them
a golden habit, stuck with many a gem:
^ pictured vesture I seem, passing by,
and thousand colors, to deceive the eye.
These fools imagining, what I present,
To be my sole and native ornament:
And therefore being enamored on my form,
If not eniov me, then they rage and storm.
But should I be before them naked laid,
And my misshapen ouglinesse displaid,
No doubt they would condemn themselves, pursuing
A seeming good, which leads them to their ruin:
The are only apt themselves to reconcile
To things their own nature base and vile.

Merc. But when it comes unto such pass that they
Are filld with wealth, and supplied every way;
When they have hedged, nay walld their riches in,
Some notwithstanding look so bare and thin,
Withal so gripple, you may sooner tear
Head from the body, than impart what is there?
Besides, it is not probable, but such
As have with greedy eyes perused thee much,
Must needly know, (however they proudly boast,
Thy outside tin-foild, or but guilt at most?

Plut. These my defaults (with others) to supply,
I have many ready helps, o Mercury.

Merc. Name them I prithee.

Plut. They no sooner fasten
With greediness upon me, but they hasten
To open their gates wide, then with me by stealth
Enter (for always they attend on wealth)
Haughtiness, Boasting, with the minds destraction,
Effoeminacie, and to make up the faction,
Oppression and Deceit, with the interest
Of thousand more; with which the heart possessed,
Is suddenly subjected and brought under,
To admire toys which are not worth the wonder,
And covet that which they ought most to fly.
Now with this band of Pensioners guarded, I
When thus attended they my state behold,
Thy never dream of other god than Gold:
with such adoration they respect me,
endure all torments, rather than reject me.

Merc. How smooth and slick thou art, no where abiding,
when men think thee safest, swiftly gliding
^row their fingers, neither can I spy
^andle or an haft to stay thee by,
we hold pots and glasses; they slip through
^ hand as snakes and serpents use to do.
When Poverty, to thee quite contrary,
^ere e're she takes her Inn is apt to tarry:
^mmy cleaves like Bird-lime, uncompeld,
^ to be seized, and easy to be held;
^ing a thousand catching hooks, and so

^ whilst we trifle here, there is one maine thing
^ had forgot.

Merc. That we did not bring
^asure along, it being Jove's intent,

(...). For that take thou no care: I do not enter

I have still a care upon my store,
my departure to shut fast my door,

Hermes well done,

A piece of mountainous and stony ground.
O wondrous! Poverty by him fast stands,
And the rough fellow Labor, with galld hands.
Here Is Wisdom, Health, and with them Fortitude,
And besides these, a populous multitude
Of such like Grooms, Need them to work compelli^
And yet a troop (methinks) thy Guard excelling.

Plut. Therefore let us post hence with what speed we
For, Hermes, how shall we invade a man

Merc. Be not afraid,
It Is Jove's command, whose will must be obayd.

Merc. To enlarge
Timon from hence; for so Jove gave in charge.

Poverty. Comes he again to Timon, whom (bereaved
Of health by many surfets) I received,
To Wisdom and to Industry commended,
And in his cure so far my skill extended,
I soon restored him (as he still does find)
Sound in his body, and upright in mind.
Have I deserved such scorn, or do I merit
A wrong, what is mine own not to inherit?
That you are come, with colorable pretense,
Him (now my sole possession) to take hence?
Whose ruined virtues with exactest care
I have much toyld and labored to repair.
Being again in that blind gods protection,
He Will bring them vassald to their late subjection,
Fill him with arrogance, disdain, and pride,
And every ill that Goodness can mis-guide;
And when all hope of fair amendment's past,
Return him back as I received him last,
Essoeminate, sloathfull, frantic, or what not,
A thing of nothing, a mere brainlesse Sot.

(...). Thou hear'st Jove's will.

Poverty. And I to it agree.
^^^wledge and Laber do you follow me,

(...). Let us be gone, o Mercury, he is mad,
^t some sad mischief from his hand be had.

Merc. This barbarous spleen good Timon strive to hide,
And thy ferocitie cast quite aside.
With gratitude receive what Jove has sent,
I strike thee luck, be rich incontinent:
Prince of the Athenians thou shall henceforth be,
And to contemn them that disdained thee,
Punish their base ingratitude, be it their grief
To see thee raised, live happy, and their Chief.

Plut. I have no need of you, pray give me leave
To use my labor, and at night receive
My competent wages, it is a gainful trade,
I have wealth enough in using this my spade:
I should be happy if you would forbear me,
But then most blessed if no man would come near me.

Merc. Thou speakst too inhumanely; Timon I
This thy harsh language and absurd reply
Will tell my father: Say that from man's breast
Thou Have had more wrongs than thou can well digest
Yet it is not good the gods thou should despise,
Who as thou see all for thy good devise.

Tim. To thee, o Mercury, Jove, and the rest
Of the Celestial gods, I here protest,
I hold my self much bound, and thank them for
Their care of me, but Plutus I abhor,
And him I Will not receive.

Merc. Why?

Tim. Because I guess
Him the fool author of my great distress
And mischiefs manifold, as first betraying me
To oily smooth-tongu'd flatterers, and then laying m^
Open to those insidiated my state.
Envy and hate he first did propagate,
Corrupted me with vices, then disclosed me
To all reproach, and after that exposed me
To spleen and cankered malice which exceeded,
And last of all left me when most I needed.
Excellent Poverty contrariwise
(...) me unto pains and exercise
^^comming Man; truly and freely we
^^gether lived in consocietie,
^pplying me with all things, garments, meat,
^^ich tasted best, being seasoned by my sweat.

^ Tyrant that lies craftily in wait:

man it is profanation to despise

Plut. Will thou, o Timon, hear me to the end,
^ilst I against thee mine own cause defend,
^^d suffer me with patience?

Timon. Speak, but briefly,
Avoiding Proems and preambles, chiefly
Used by damned Orators: see thou be'st short,
I Will listen to thee, but thank Hermes for it.

Plut. More liberty by right I ought to claim,
Whom thou of wrongs injuriously do blame;
Thy invective is with bitterness extended,
Yet innocent I in nothing have offended,
Who thee of all delicious things provided,
At thy free will to be disposed and guided:
I was the author and chief instrument
Of thy authority and government;
I gave thee crowns, and furnished thee with treasure,
Made thee conspicuous, to abound in pleasure.
In all rarieties I thee instated:
By me thou were observed, and celebrated.
If since, ought ill have unto thee betided,
(Cause thou perhaps my goodness have misguided)
By seeming friends or servants, can thou blame
Plutus for this? I rather should exclaim
On thee, for many contumelies past,
Pouring me out among sordid knaves so fast:
Who only sweld thee with vainglorious pride,
Devising strange prestigious tricks beside,
Only to draw me from thee. In The last place
Where thou have uttered to my foul disgrace,
I left thee in thy want to starve and pine,
Be witness Hermes if the fault were mine:
Who after injuries not to be borne,
Did cast me from thee in contempt and scorn,
Hence comes it, for thy cloak of purple die,
Thy late beloved Mistress Poverty
Has wrapped thee in this skin coat. I attest
Thee, Mercury, how much I was oppressed:
And but that Jove commands, by no facility
Could I be won to atone this our hostility.

Merc. But Plutus thou now find'st how he is changed,
and from his former humor quite estranged.
therefore have free commerce, dig Timon still,
and in the mean time Plutus use thy skill,
that as by Jove's behest thou art assigned,
^ delving deep he may this treasure find.

Timon. Well Hermes, I obey, and am prepared
^o be again made rich: For man it is hard
^o wrestle with the gods. Observe, I'ntreat,
^^to what miseries and mischiefs great
^hou have headlong cast me, who (I vow) until

that ill have I deserved, now to be vexed,

Merc. And yet take

^are it, however weighty and indeed

Plut. He Is mounted, having left us, making way
^ith his swift wings: but thou, o Timon, stay

^t strike hard, harder yet; and now to thee

^ve from the Earths deep intrals brought aloft
^y hidden luster, and here coucht thee soft
^on this grassy verdure. O Jove, father
Of prodigies, or what we else may gather
From thy Divine Power: o my dearest friends
The (f) Caribanthes, how your love extends?
And thou light-bearing Mercury, behold,
And freely tell me, Whence is all this gold?
It is some dream, I am deceived, I fear,
These are quick glowing coals new waked here.
No sure, it is excellent gold yellow and bright,
Most ravishing, all-pleasing to the sight,
Beautiful Coin: O let me hug thee then,
Thou art the goddess of Good-lucke to men:
It flames like fire compact, in this huge cluster
Both night and day it keeps it is glorious luster.
Approach to me my Dearest, how to miss thee
I know not now: Most Amorous let me kiss thee.
Till now I did not credit what was told
Long since, That Jove himself was changed to gold.
What precise Virgin could retain the power
Not to hold up to such a golden shower?
Or being the chastest of all humane daughters,
Not meet him dropping through the tiles and rafters.
Take Midas, Croesus, and the Magozine
Heapt by the offerings made at Delphos shrine;
Compared with this Mass they are nothing to it,
And take the Persian Monarchy to boot.
O Spade, o Skin-coat, late to me most dear,
To Pan the rural god I leave you here.
I Will buy a field remote hence, and obscure,
Where having built a strong tower to secure.
This mountainous heap, I Will study (being gone)
How I may best live to my self alone.
There will I build my tomb too, e're I dye,
That none may know where Timons ashes lie.
I have decreed, and it is established in me,
That none from this sequestered life shall win me,
Nor hate against all mankind. Henceforth a guest,
A friend, or a companion, I protest,
Are names forgot in me: The Altar of Pity,
So much esteemed and honored in the City,
I Will hold as a mere trifle. Commiseration
On those that grieve or make loud acclamation,
To give the Needy, or their wants supply,
Shall be to me as black iniquity.
Subversion of good manners I Will allow,
A sad and solitary life I vow,
Such as Wolves lead, bloodthirsty to the end,
For only Timon shall be Timons friend;
All else my foes, with whom I am at strife,
As those that still insidiate my life:
To intercourse with any that has bin
Before my friend, I le hold a capital sin,
Deserving expiation: and the day
I hat I encounter Kinsman in my way,
I Will think unprosp'rous: for no more I pass
For Man, than statues made of stone or brass;
With such I Will hold no covenant. Solitude
Be thou mine aim and end: as for those rude
Of mine own Tribe, Cousins and Nephews, or
Mine own domesticke servants I abhor;
My Country likewise: I to all their shames
shall count them as mere cold and barren names.
The are mad men's Saints, but trifles to the Wise;
Be thou alone rich, Timon, and despise
All else: Thy self on y thy self delight,
And separated live from the loathed sight
Of Sycophants, (the remnant of thy days)
Who only swell thee up with tympanous praise.
Offer thy gifts unto the go^s alone,
Feast with thy self, be thine own neighbor, none
Near thee: whatever is thine partic pate
Unto thy proper ends, 'nd Rivals hate.
It likewise is decreed, That Timon will
Himself use gently and humanely still,
Be his own page and servant, when his breath
Leaves him, his own eyes he will close in death.
If love vainglory, he will himself renown;
On his own head his own hand place a crown:
No style of honor be to him so sweet,
As to be called Misanthropos, it is meet,
Because he hates Mankind: the Character
That in all ages I desire to wear,
Is Difficulty and Asperitie,
Fierceness, Rage, Wrath, and Inhumanitie:
For should I see a poor wretch wrapped in fire,
And he to quench him should my help desire,
I would but laugh to see him fry and broile,
Seeking to feed the flame with pitch and oil.
Again, f passing by a rivers brinke,
And spying one fallen in, ready to sink,
And holding out his hand imploring aid,
Craving to be supported up and staid;
What in this case think you would Timon do?
Even dive his head down to the bottom too.
There are no other laws confirmed, than these,
By Timon, son to Echecratides,
Even Timon of Collytte, with his hand
Subscribes to them, which he will not countermand.
O now at what a dear rate would I buy,
That present news might into Athens fly,
And all of them upon the sudden know
What store I have, how little to bestow.
What noise was that? See, multitudes come posting,
Clouded in dust, and breathless, this way coasting?
I wonder how they smelt my gold? Were It best
I clime up to yon hill, from whose high crest
I with more ease with stones may palt them hence?
Or shall I rather for this once dispense
With my harsh laws? to show them all my store,
With the bare sight thereof to vex them more?
I hold that best; their coming here I Will stay:
But soft, what is he that is foremost on the way?
Gnatonides the Flatterer, who but late
When I was in my miserable estate,
And begged of him some food for charity,
Cast me an halter: yet ungrateful he
A thousand times has at my table eaten,
I am glad yet he comes first, first to be beaten.

Gnaton. Did I not ever think the gods above
Could not neglect, but still this good man love?
Hail Timon, thou most fair, most sweet, most kind,
Bounteous, and always of a generous mind.

Tim. Hail too Gnatonides, (the corruptest slave
That ever gourmandis'd) what would thou have,
Thou more than many Vultures still devouring?

Gnaton. It was his custom always to be pouring
Harsh jeasts upon his friends; his quick dicacitie
Would evermore be taunting my voracitie,
And it becomes him well. Where shall we dine,
Or whether go to quaffe thy health in wine?
I have a new song got into my pate,
Out of quaint (p) Dythirambs I learned it late.

Timon. But at this time I rather could advise
That thou would study doleful Elegies,
Such as this spade can teach.

Gnaton. O Hercules!
Strikes Timon then? with thee, I witness these,
Before the Arcopagitae I
Will have thee called in Court: o I shall die,
See, thou have wounded me.

Timon. Nay be not gone;
Two labors thou may save me so in one:
Thou shall complain of murder.

Gnat. Timon No:
But rather on my broken pate bestow
Some of thy gold to apply to it, and be sure,
It Is both a speedy and miraculous Cure.

Tim. Still stay'st thou?

Gnat. I am gone. Wondering he is grown
Of late so rude, that was so civil known.

Tim Who Is he comes next, all bare and bald before?

Philiades: I know him of the store
Of Sycophants most execrable, who wound
Me in not long since for a piece of ground,
Besides two talents for his daughters dower,
And all that substance did the slave devour,
Because he praised my singing: when the rest
Were silent all, he only did protest
And swear, that I did admiration breed,
Nay, dying Swans in sweetness much exceed.
I since being sick, desiring him to have care
Over my health, the Villein did not spare
To spurn me from his gate.

Philiades. Ungrateful age,
Do thou at length know Timon, he, the sage
And wise good man: full well did he requite
Gnatonides the soothing Parasite,
And Temporiser, who is only friend
To such as of their wealth can know no end.
But he has what he merits, a just fate
Depending on the Unthankful and Ingrate:
But we that have been table-guests of old,
Equals, and fellow Citisens, enrold;
Who' twixt us interchang'd the name of brother,
And were not chargeable one to another,
We should renew acquaintance: Sir, God save you,
And beware henceforth how you do be have you
To sacrilegious Parasits that appear
Always at banquets and abundant cheer:
They are only Smell-feasts, waiting on the Cooks,
But little differing from base Crows and Rooks,
Men are of late so'bnoxious unto crimes,
^here is no trust to any of these times;
^nthankfull they are all, and bad: but I
^nowing thy wants, and willing to supply
^hy present uses, purposed to have brought
^ talent with me; fearing thou had owght
To some harsh Creditor; or might have need
^or other ends: but by the way indeed,
learing to what a surplusage of gain,
^hou have arrived, I held it a thing vain.
^et came I of thy bounty to make proof,
and counsel thee of things for thy behoof:
^ut needless were it, Timon being so wise,
That (if he lived) he Nestor might advise.

Tim 'Twas kindly done, Philiades, come near
and see what welcome I have for thee here.

Phil. Thou wretched churl; what undeserved punishment
^ast thou repaid me for my late admonishment?
^^ fear he has broke my neck.

Tim. Behold a third,
Demeas the Orator; indeed a Bird
Of the same feather: he has bills, records,
^ables, a man merely composed of words.
^e calls himself my kinsman; who in one day
Of mine) to the Cities Chamber had to pay
^xteen whole talents, he then in execution:
^et I redeemed him, and made full solution
Of all his debts; when he was fast in hold,
^freed him thence: yet was the slave so bold,
That coming after unto eminent place,
Where he with Erichtheiades had grace,
Who had the charge of the whole Treasury,
and money by account then due to me)
^e being my feed Advocate as then,
^rotested that I was no Citisen;
Therefore not capable my due to claim:
Most loudly lying without fear or shame.

Demeas. Save thee, o Timon, thou, of all thy race
The greatest ornament and the prime grace,
Of the whole State the Column and the stay,
By whom protected and supported, they
Live safe: thou art the stay of Greece, we know,
The people frequently pronounce thee so,
With either Court: but hear what I have writ
In thy great praise, and then consider it.
Timon, of Echecratides the son,
Borne in Collytte, who has never don
But what became him well; who as he was
Of unstained life, in wisdom did surpass
The Grecian Sages; who from himself did steal
His precious hours, to benefit the Weal.
He was so good a Patriot, besides strong,
And from the Olympicke wrestling brought along
Great honors by his swiftness, by his force,
The four wheeld Chariot and the single horse.

Tim. I have not so much as spectator bin
Of what thou say I am so eminent in.

Demeas. All Is one for that, we Orators are free,
And what is not yet done may hereafter be:
These are but things of course, and aptly fitted,
I see no reason they should be omitted.
But the last year, no longer since, how well
Did he demean himself, nay how excel,
When he against the Achernenses fought,
And their great army unto ruin brought?
The Spartans in two battles he subdued.

Timon. How can these be? Do not my sense delude:
I never being soldier, nor had mind,
Or the least purpose to be so inclined.

Demeas. It Is modesty in you, I must confess,
To be so sparing of your worthiness.
But as for us, we should be most ingrate,
^ we your great worth did not celebrate.
^esides, in Laws, which (truly understood)
^ave been inacted for the public good;
^n private consultations about war
Or peace, he did transcend all others far,
And brought unto the public State such profit,
That there is none can speak too loudly of it.
^or these just causes it is held convenient,
and by the Lords and Commons thought expedient,
Being a man so generally respected)
To have a golden statue erected
To this great Commonwealths man Timon, graced
^o far, as to be next Minerva placed,
^n her own Temple, shaking in his hand
As imitating Jove) a fulminous brand,
Bright rays about his head, and at the least,
Decked with seven Crowns, to have his name inereast.
Next, to have all his glories open laid
^n the new Tragedies to Bacchus made.
These solemn Sacreds must be kept this day,
And who more fit than he to act them, pray?
^emect to this decree does first subscribe,
Because he counts himself of Timons tribe,
His near Ally and kinsman, or indeed
His scholar rather, for he does exceed
^n learning the superlative degree,
As being all what he can wish to be.
This is the general suffrage, and thy due:
But how had I forgot? that to thy view
I did not bring my son and heir, the same
Whom I have since called Timon, by thy name.

Tim. How can that be, o Demeas, when thou have
No wife at all, pretending to live chaste?
Thou art a Bachelor.

Them. Tush, do not fear,
My purpose is to marry the next year.
If heaven permit, and thou shall hear relation,
That all my study shall be procreation.
Then my first Borne (a boy it shall be sure)
I Will Timon call, to make thy name endure.

Tim. But if in this sad stroke I not miscarry,
'Twill be a doubt if ever thou shall marry.

Demeas. O me, what means this outrage? art thou wise
That do upon thy friends thus tyrannise?
To beat him hence, that has more quick conceit
And apprehension in this broken pate,
Than thou in thy great Mazard: neither can
This justify thee for an honest man,
Or a good Citisen: This outrage don,
Shall question thee before the setting Sun;
For I dare justify, thou durst aspire
To set the Cities Citadel on fire.

Tim. That calumny will to thine own shame turn,
Because the place has not been seen to burn.

Them. But being rich, it may suspected be,
That thou have robbed the common Treasury.

Tim. The bolts and locks are whole, and 'twill appear
Most vile to such as shall thy scandals hear.

Them. It may be robbed hereafter; in the mean time
Thou thus possessed art guilty of that crime.

Tim. Mean time take that, 'twill speed thee if 't hit righ

Them. O me; that blow 'twixt neck and shoulders light

Tim. Sh^eeke not so loud, o Demeas, if thou do,
Here Is a third for thee. Methinks it were most
Ridiculous, that being unweapon'd, I
Two mighty Spartan armies made to fly,
And one poor snake not vanquish: so in vain
The honors from Olympus I should gain,
To championise and wrestle. Soft, what is he?
Grave Thrasicles the Sophist it should be:
The same; I know him by his promise beard,
And beetle brows: Some things that are not heard
^^ mutters to himself, and his squint eye
^sts toward the Moon, as should his wits there lie:
^s unshorne hair beneath his shoulders flowing,
about him scattered with continual blowing:
^ke Boreas or some Tryton he appears;
^t such as Zeuxes (since not many years)
^ tables used to figure them. Now he,
^ habit rare and thin, makes toward me,
^cing a modest, but affected gate,
^^ if he had new crochets in his pate.
^e ^useth too: wonder you would to hear
in every morning, with a look austere,
^^spute of Virtue and her excellent quality,
^eproving all delights, only frugalitie,
Which he affects) extolling. His first care
^ first to wash, then instantly prepare
^imselfe to meat, but at some others charge.
^^ soon as set, the boy brings him a large
and brim-filld bowl; no liquor him can escape,
^ it be strong and pressed from the pure Grape,
^ike Lethe's water, down the wine he poures
^is yawning throat; talks, At his early hours
that his Positions were and Disputations;
^roubling the hearers with his vain narrations.
^ow he begins to gourmandise, and sits
^ouering upon the choice and fattest bits,
As if the table could not room afford)
^e strikes his neighbors elbow from the board,
^ earnest feeding; crumbs hang on his beard;
^ith several saucers all his chaps are smeared.
^eing almost gorged, upon the fruits he flies,
and almost grovelling over the platters lies;
^umbling and searching with insatiat mind,
^s if in them he virtue hoped to find.
^ith his long finger having scraped the dish,
and slapt up all the sauce of flesh or fish,
So clean, that not a waiter, sparelier fed,
Shall have ought left wherein to dip his bread:
Still sits he as his greasy fists have shaped him,
Vexed, that some glorious morsel has escaped him;
Though he alone whole custards has devoured,
And his wide throat with tarts and marchpanes scoured
Yet he is not satisfied, although at least
He has gormandiz'd a whole hog at a feast.
Now the best fruits that grow from this voracitie,
Is to be loud, and prate with great audacitie.
His guts full stuffed, and brains well toxt with wine,
Himself he spruceth, studieth to be fine;
Either prepares his squealing voice to sing,
Or dancing, hops about as he would fling
His gouty legs off from his rotten thighs.
Wearied with these, again he does devise
Of new discourse, and that must chiefly be
Of temperance and grave sobriety.
Now is he made a sport to all the Board,
Stammers and lisps, speaks not a ready word;
Then drinks even unto vomit: Last of all,
To take the nasty fellow thence they call.
Then there is with both hands lifting; loath he leaves
The place, and unto some she Minstrell cleaves,
Ready to ravish her in all their view,
To show that Lust does Drunkenness pursue;
Nay in his best sobriety applying
Himself to boldness, avarice, and lying;
In which none can out-match him, he is a Chief
Both with the soothing flatterer and Thief:
For perjurie there is no man that transcends him,
Imposture ushers, Impudence attends him.
He is an Object of mere observation,
Or (truly looked into) of admiration;
A spectacle of scorn, that wonder brings,
Being made complete from mere imperfect things:
^ll his imperfections, more or less,
^ming a kind of modesty to express.
Most strange! O Thrasicles, What make you here?

Thrasicles. Not with the mind of others I appear,
Timon, who come flocking to behold
^ee and thy mighty Magozin of gold,
^^^haps to steal and pilpher, to be guests
^usive to thy table and thy feasts;
^o daub thee with pyde flatteries, that indeed
^^ a man simple, and do Counsel need;
^rainlesse Prodigal, wholly given to wast,
^ily parting with what coin thou have.
Besides, thou art not ignorant, I am sure,
^at spare and thirfty diet I endure,
^e Chop or Fragment best with me agreeing,
^n just so much as will maintain a Being:
^^ onion is a meat to taste my pallat,
^ a few water Cresses a choice sallat;
^^ttle salt cast on them, then it is rare,
^ I account it most delicious fare.
^ thirst the Athenian fountain sates and fills,
^ich by seven cocks it plenteously distills.
^s threadbare cloak by me is prizd more hye
^an the best robe dipped in the Tyrian dye:
^ Gold, thou know that I esteem't no more
^n I do pebbles scattered on the shore.
^et for thy sake I hither made access,
^ing thy wealth, thy goodness might oppress;
^^g corrupt and vile in it is own being,
^ no way with thy temperature agreeing,
^ rout of irrecoverable ills,
^^ich seeming most to comfort, soonest kills.
^ul'd by me, Go instantly and cast
^ the Ocean all the wealth thou have:
at need of Gold, when all things we supply
contemplation of Philosophy?
But cast it not into the depth I prethes,
But near the shore, when only I am with thee;
Enough it is if the wave but overflow it,
To cover it, and (save my self) none know it.
If this dislike thee, that thou holdst it vain,
I have another project in my brain,
And 't may prove the best course; From forth thy do^^
Precipitate and tumble all thy store;
And to express a pure abstemious mind,
Of all thy Mass leave not a piece behind.
There is a third way (like the second) speedy,
Namely, by distributing to the needy;
Who in all ears shall thy donation sound:
To him five drams, give that man a pound,
A talent to another. If by chance,
Philosophers of austere countenance
Hither to taste thy largess shall repair,
Give such a double, nay a treble share,
As to the men most worthy. This (alas)
I for mine own part speak not, but to pass
Thy bounty unto others that more need,
And would be thankful, of thy gift to feed.
For my particular use I crave no more
Than so much at this present from thy store
As would but fill my Scrip, the bulk being small,
Holds two Aegina bushels, and that is all:
To be content with little, moderation
And temperance becomes men of my fashion:
We Sophists, that in wisdom all outstrip,
Should aim at nothing further than our Scrip.

Tim. All that thou speakst I (Thrasicles) allow;
Yet e're I fill thy wallet, hear me now,
I Will stuff thy head with tumors, having made
True measure of thy skull with this my spade.

Thrasic. O Liberty! o Laws! near a free City,
Thus to be used by one devoid of pity!

Tim. Why, Thrasicles, thus angry do thou show thee?
^ave I not paid thee the full debt I owe thee?
^ay but a little, and to express my love,
^ure measures thou shall have over and above.
that further business have we now in breeding?
^ultitudes hither flock, in throngs exceeding;
^ere's Blepsias, Laches, Cniphon, and in brief,
thousand more that hasten to their grief,
^ if they ran for blows; see how they flock:
therefore I Will clyme to the highest part of this rock,
^old that course is for the present best,
and to my wearied spade to give some rest:
^f scattered stones I Will gather me an heap,
and from that place I Will make them skip and leap,
^uring my hail on them.

Bleps. Hurl not, we pray,
Timon, instantly we will trudge away.

Tim. And yet thou shall with difficulty do it,
without some bloodshed and deep wounds to boot.

The Argument of the Dialogue entitled IVPITER and GANIMEDE.
Jove's Masculine love this Fable reprehends,
And wanton dotage on the Trojan Boy.
Shaped like an Eagle, he from the earth ascends,
And bears through the air his new Delight and Joy.
In Ganimed's expressed a simple Swain,
Who would leave Heaven, to live on Earth again.
The DIALOGVE.
Jupiter. NOw kiss me, lovely Ganimed, for see,
We are at length arrived where w^ would be:
I have no crooked beak, no tallons ke^
No wings or feathers are about me seen;
I am not such as I but late appeared.

Ganimed. But were not you that Eagle who late feared
And snatched me from my flock? where is become
That shape? you speak now, who but late were dumb

Iupit. I am no man, fair Youth, as I appear,
Nor Eagle, to astonish thee with fear:
But King of all the gods, who for some reason
Have by my power transhap't me for a season.

Ganim. What Is that you say? you are not Pan, I know:
^here's the^ your pipe? or where your horns, should grow
^on your temples? where your hairy thighs?

Jupiter. Thinks Ganimed that godhood only lies rural Pan?

Ganim. Why not? I know him one:
^e Shepherds sacrifice to him alone.
spotted Goat into some cave we drive,
^d then he seiseth on the beast alive.
^ou art but some Childe-stealer, that is thy best.

Iupit. Have thou not heard of any man contest
Jove's great Name? nor his rich Altar viewed
Gargarus, with plenteous showers bedewed?
here seen his fire and thunder?

Ganim. Do you then
^ffirme your self the same, who on us men
^ late poured haile-stones? he that dwells above us,
^^d there makes noise; yet some will say does love us?
^ whom my Father did observance yield,
^^d sacrificed the best Ram in the field.
^^y then (if you of all the gods be chief)
^^ve you, by stealing me, thus played the thief;
^^en in my absence the poor sheep may stray,
the wild ravenous Wolves snatch them away?

Iupit. Yet have thou care of Lambs, of Folds, of sheep,
^^at now art made immortal, and must keep ^ietie with Us?

Ganim. I no way can
^^nceive you. Will you play the honest man, ^^d bear me back to Ida?

(...). So in vain
^ap'd me like an Eagle, if again ^ould return thee back.

Ganim. My father, he
this has made inquiry after me; ^^d if the least of all the flock be eaten,
I in his rage am most sure to be beat.

Iup. Where shall he find thee?

Ganim. That Is the thing I fear,
He never can clime up to meet me here,
But if thou be a good god, let me pass
Into the mount of Ida where I was:
And then I le offer, in my thankful piety,
Another well fed Goat unto thy diety,
(As price of my redemption) three years old,
And now the chief and prime in all the fold.

Iup. How simple is this innocent Lad? a mere
Innocuous child. But Ganimed now hear.
Bury the thoughts of all such terren dross,
Think Ida and thy fathers flocks no loss:
Thou now art heavenly, and much grace may do
Unto thy father and thy country too.
No more of cheese and milk from henceforth think,
Ambrosia thou shall eat, and Nectar drink,
Which thy fair hands in flowing cups shall fill
To me and others, but atte^d us still;
And (that which most should moove thee) make thy' abo^
Where thou art now, thou shall be made a god,
No more be mortal, and thy glorious star
Shine with refulgence, and be seen from far.
Here thou art ever happy.

Ganim. But I pray,
When I would sport me; who is here to play?
For when in Ida I did call for any,
Both of my age and growth it yielded many.

Iup. Play-fellowes for thee I will likewise find,
Cupid, with diverse others to thy mind,
And such as are both of thy years and sise,
To sport with thee all what thou can devise:
Only be bold and pleasant, and then know
Thou shall have need of nothing that is below.

Ganim. But here no service I can do indeed,
Unless in heaven you had some flocks to feed.

Iup. Yes, thou to me shall fill celestial wine,
and wait upon me when in state I dine:
^hen learn to serve in banquets.

Ganim That I can
^ready, without help of any man:
^r I use ever when we dine or sup,
^o pour out milk, and crown the pastoral cup.

Iup. Fie, how thou still remember'st milk and beasts,
if thou were to serve at mortal Feasts:
^ow, this is heaven, be merry then and laugh;
^hen thou art thirsty thou shall Nectar quaffe.

Ganim. Is it so sweet as milk?

(...) Pris'd far before,
which tasted once, milk thou will ask no more.

Ganim. Where shall I sleep a nights? what, must I ly
^^h my companion Cupid?

(...). So then I
^vaine had raped thee: but I from thy sheep
^ purpose stole thee, by my side to sleep.

Ganim. Can you not lie alone? but will your rest
^me sweeter, if I nuzzle on your breast?

(...). Yes, being a child so fair:

Ganim. How can you think
beauty, whilst you close your eyes and wink?

(...). It is a sweet inticement, to increase
^^ntented rest, when our desire's at peace.

Ganim. I, but my father every morn would chide,
^^d say, those nights he lodged me by his side
^uch disturbed his rest; tumbling and ^ossing
^wart the bed, my little legs still crossing
^ either kicking this way, that way sprawling,
^f he but removed me, straitwaies yawling:
^^ grumbling in my dreams, (for so he sed)
often times sent me to my mothers bed:
then would she complain upon me worse.
Then if for that you stole me, the best course
Is even to send me back again; for I
Am ever so unruly where I lie,
Wallowing and tumbling, and such coile I keep,
That I shall but disturb you in your sleep.

Iupit. In that the greater pleasure I shall take,
Because I love still to be kept awake.
I shall embrace and kiss thee then the ofter,
And by that means my bed seem much the softer.

Ganim. But whilst you wake I Will sleep.

Iup. Mercury, see
This Lad straight taste of immortality;
And making him of service capable,
Let him be brought to wait on us at table.

IVPITER and IVNO.
The Argument of the Dialogue.
IVno of Ganimed is jealous grown,
And much vpbraids Jove with the Phrygian Swain;
^illing (before him) to prefer her own:
^^d therefore blames her husband, but in vain.
Although this Fable to the gods extends,
Base sordid lust in man it reprehends.
The DIALOGUE.
^^^o. SInce this young Trojan Swain to heaven thou have brought,
O Jupiter, thou set'st thy Wife at naught.

Jupit. Of him too art thou jealous, a poor Swain,
^ough beautiful, yet innocent and plain?
^s in hope thou only had a spleen
women, such as I before have been
^^miliar with.

(...). Nor have thou made expression
thy great deity in such transgression,
^r done such things as have thee well beseem'd;
^o being a god above the rest esteemed,
Descendest down to earth, making it full
Of thy Adulteries: some imes like a Bull;
Then like a golden Shower, and keeping still
Those Prostitutes below to s^te thy will.
But now again, Thou, mightiest of the dieties,
Lest that there should be end of thy impieties;
Being now inflamed with an unheard desire,
Have this young Phrygian Lad snatched from his Sire,
Brought hither to out-brave me, and set ods
Betwixt us, filling Nect^r to the gods.
Is there such want of Cup bearers? or weary
Is Hebe yet, or Vulcan, to make merry
Thy Gu^sts inv^^ed? that no so^ner thou
Take from his hand the bowl, but straight to bow
And kiss his sweet lip, nay in all our sight:
In that kiss seeming to take more delight,
Than in the Nectar drunk: but which is worst,
Often callst for drink when there is no cause of thirst;
And as in sport (but sipping) thy arm stretchest,
And the full Chalice to the Wanton reachest,
And he but tasting, as shall please him best,
Then to his health carowsest all the rest;
And in the same place where his lip did touch,
Thou take thy draught, thy lewd desire is such,
With heedfulnesse and care noting the brim,
So, at once kissing both the cup and him.
Not long since too, this King and potent Father
Of men and all mortality, the rather
To sport with him, his Scepter laid aside,
And thunders, with which late he terrify'de
The lower world. And speak, was not this wrong
To a Brow so great? a Beard so full and long?
All this I have seen, all these I have endured,
And nothing's done that is to me obscured.

Jupiter. Why's this to thee so grievous, o my wife,
That it should raise betwixt us the least strife?
That a young Lad, so f^ire and sweet as this,
should please me both with Nectar and a kiss?
^houldst thou but taste those lips (which I am Ioth)
^hou would not blame me to prefer them both
before all Nectar and Ambrosia too;
^ay, if thou did, even so thy self would do.

Juno. These are the words of masculine love, much hated,
^^ or am I mad, to be degenerated
^ base effeminacies as to take delight
^ the loathed kisses of a Catamite.

Iup. Pray (you most generous) do not so deprave
those loves and pleasures I am pleased to have:
^his pretty sweet effeminat Lad to me
^ dearer far -- but I Will not anger thee.

Juno. I wish in my place you had that Lad wedded,
^ith whom you ofter than with me have bedded
^nce his arrive: your loathed wife shall bethink her,
^ow better to behave her toward your Skinker.

Iup. Is It only fit, Vulcan thy son should fill
^ectar, who being lame is apt to spell;
and bluntly running from the furnace, smells
^ smoke, dust, sweat, and what I know not else,
^ith sparks scarce quenched, before the gods to stand,
^s sooty tongs new laid out of his hand,
^o take from him the goblet? which being done,
^o embrace, then kiss thy most deformed son;
whom scarcely thou his mother would so grace,
^aring his smudg'd lips should begrime thy face.
^he that only sweet Youth must adorn
^he gods high banquets, being made their scorn?
^^d therefore must this Phrygian be confined,
^^cause he is clear in looks, as pure in mind?
those face so smooth, whose tongue does so excel,
^^d in all points becomes the place so well.
^t that which most torments thee, since his kiss
any degrees more sweet than Nectar is:

Juno. Now Vulcan unto thee (o Jove) seems lame,
His forge, his apron, tongs, and tools, thy shame:
What nastinesse? What loathsomeness? but he
Now at this instant does appear to thee
Infected with; whilst thou before thee have
That fair faced Trojan Lad? but in times past,
None of this foul deformity was seen,
No sparks, no soot, no dust to move thy spleen:
His furnace in those days did not affright thee,
But then his filling Nectar much delight thee.

Iupit. Thou make thy self sick of thine old disease,
O Juno, and this Trojan does more please,
Because of him thou art jealous: if thou scorn
From him to take the Cup; of thy self borne
Thou have to fill thee, Vulcan, one so smug,
As if he gaped still for his mothers dug.
But thou, o Ganimed, to me alone
Reach the rich bowl. Two kisses for that one
I Will give thee still, when I receive it first,
And when return it, having quenched my thirst,
Why weep'st thou? fear not, they that mean thee harm
Mischief are sure to taste. Sweet boy thine arm.

IVPITER and CUPID.
The Argument.
GReat Jupiter on wanton Love has seized,
Ripping up injuries before time done;
And hardly is the Thunderers rage appeased,
^ut holds him fast that is about to run.
^he childish wag submissive language uses,
and with what art he can himself excuses.
The DIALOGVE.
Cupid. WHerein have I, o Jupiter, transgrest;
That by thy power I should be thus oppressed?
Being a child, and therefore simple?

Jupiter. Thou
^ child at these years, Cupid? who I vow,
^rt older than Iapetus, hop'st thou to win
^avor, because no hair upon thy chin
^ppeares? and thou art beardlesse? but beguild
^ust we be still in holding thee a child?
^eing both old and crasie?

Cup. I pray tell
^his subtle old man, whom you know so well,
that wrong he'hath done, that you would bind him?

Iup. See,
Thou wretch, do think it a small injury,
To make me such a mockery and a jest
To all men: that a god should to a beast
Transhape himself: into a Satyr, than
Into a Bull, an Eagle, and a Swan:
Next to a golden Shower? all these thou have made me.
But that wherein thou chiefly have betrayed me,
My will by force or slight I must obtain,
But never love, to be beloved again:
Nor by thy power have I more gracious been
To my wife Juno the celestial Queen;
But forced to use prestigious strange disguise,
In all my scapes to hide me from her eyes.
Besides, our mutual pleasures are not full,
They only kiss an Eagle or a Bull:
But should I in my personal shape appear,
Even at my sight (poor things) they die with fear.

Cupid. That only shows thy power and divine might,
Since mortal eyes cannot endure thy sight.

Iup. How comes it, Hyacinthus is so dear,
And Branchus. to Apollo? Is his Sphere
More bright than ours? yet they about him cling,
In his own shape.

Cup. But Daphne that coy thing,
Though he showed young and beardlesse, his cheeks red,
And each way lovely, his embraces fled.
If love then would be amorous, and apply
Himself to Love, his shield he must lay by,
And fearful thunders, smoothly kembe his hair,
And part it both ways, to appear more fair:
Wear on h^s head a Chaplet for a Crown,
And flowing from his shoulders a loose gown
Died in Sidonian purple: on his feet
Sandals, whose the with golden buckles meet:
Unto the Pipe and Timbrell learn to dance,
And foot it to them finely: so by chance
More glorious Beauties may to him incline,
Than Menades attend the god of Wine.

Iup. Away: I more esteem my regal state,
Than to appear so poorly effeminate:

Cup. Love not at all, and that is more easy far.

Iup. Yes, love I must, whilst here such Beauties ar,
And gain them with less trouble, mauger thee.
^o for this time be gone.

Cup. I now am free.

VULCAN and APOLLO
The Argument.
'Twixt Vulcan and Apollo speech is held
Of young Cillenius, Maia's newborn son;
How he in cheats and theevings has excelled:
Relating strange things in his cradle done.
Since whom, all infants borne beneath his star,
In craft and guile exceed all others far.
The DIALOGVE.
Vulcan. Have thou not seen (Apollo) the young Brat^
So late brought forth by lovely Maia? tha^
Looks in his swathes so beautifully fair
Snarling on all such as about him are;
Whom no one that beholds him, but surmises
That he is borne for some great enterprises?

Apollo. Shall I (o Vulcan) him an infant call?
Or think him borne for any good at all?
Who for his craft and subtlety (I vow)
Is than Iapetus older.

Vulcan. Tell me how?
What wrong can this young Baby do, I pray,
Who came into the world but yesterday?

Apollo. Ask Neptune that, whose Trident he has stolen:
Demand of Mars, (with rage and anger swollen)
Whether his brain least subtlety afford?
Out of whose scabberd he has stolen his sword?
Or let me speak what by my self I know:
From me unwares my quiver and my bow
He slily snatched.

Vulcan. How can it be, his hands
Being tied up so close in swathing bands.

Apollo. Yet be not thou too confident, I entreat thee,
For come he near thy shop, he will likewise heat thee.

Vulcan. He was with me but now.

Apollo. Do thou misdoubt thee
Of nothing lost? have all thy tools about thee?
What, not one wanting?

Vulc. None.

Apollo. Free from his wrongs
Art thou alone?

Vulc. By Jove I miss my tongs,
They Are stolen out of my forge.

Apoll. These thou shall find
About him hid, do but his swathes unbinde.

Vulc. Has he such catching fingers? (past believing)
^ure in his mothers womb he studied theeving.

Apollo. Did thou not hear him, Vulcan, talk and prate
With voluble tongue, and phrases accurate?
Now in his infancy, so young, so small,
Offering to be a servant to us all.
^o sooner borne, but Cupid he did dare
To try a fall with him, and threw him fair.
^im Venus for his victory embraced,
^or which he steals her girdle from her wast.
Jove smiling at the theft, and therewith pleased,
^ean time the crafty wag his Scepter seized:
^o steal his Trisulke he had made a shift,
^ut 'twas too heavy for his strength to lift.

Vulc. Thou telst me of a Lad active and daring,
A nimble juggling Jack.

Apollo. Nay, he is not sparing
To profess Music too.

vulc. How is that known?

Apoll. The invention too he seeks to make his own:
Having the shell of a dead Tortoise found,
He makes an instrument thereof for sound;
To which a crooked neck he first made fast,
Boring therein round holes, and in them placed
Pinnes to wind up the cords by: to the Shells back
A belly frames: seven strings, which he does slack,
And sometimes stretch, he fixes; which but touch,
They yield a sweet sound that delights much.
Whose notes I envy, be they flat or sharp.
Since he contends to exceed me in my Harp.
Even Maia's self I often have heard complain,
She cannot in the heavens her son contain:
His ever-waking brain, in action still,
Can take no rest: by night (against her will)
In silence he conveyes himself to hell,
Whether to steal ought thence she cannot tell.
Besides, he has wings, a Caducaeus too
Of a miraculous power, and force to do
Things wonderful, by which he can bestow
Souls hence departed, in the fields below,
Or thence convey them hither.

Vulc. Most sure I will
Add something to encourage his rare skill.

Apoll. Which he has well requited; for to day
(No longer since) he stole thy tongs away.

Vulc. 'Twas well done to remember me of this,
Because my tongs are tools I cannot miss.
Somewhere about him they are still, no doubt:
But first the fire I Will in my forge put out.

MERCVRY and APOLLO.
The Argument.
OF Jove and of Alcmena: The long night
In which the great Alcides was begot,
^his Fable speaks. And if I guess aright,
(...) this the Author much profaned not,
To tax the heathen Idols his pretense is,
Since men are punished for the gods offenses.
The DIALOGVE.
Mercury. TO thee, o Phoebus, Jupiter does say,
Forbear to mount thy Chariot for this day;
The next too, and the third, disclose no light,
^t for that time make it continual night.
^^epe in, command the Hours thy steeds to untrace,
and thy bright Sun beams pluck from off thy face.
^r, without intermission being oppressed
^ith such long pains, it is fit thou should have rest,

Apollo. Thou telst me a new thing, unheard till now;
^^ve I transgrest my course, or been too slow,
^^ over-swift? that Jove should prove a way
^^ make the night thrice longer than the day.
There Is no such thing; he only has intent
At some one aim on which his mind is bent,
And this time only (but not still to be)
To have this one night made as long as three.

Apollo. Where is he now, or from whence art thou sent To tell me this?

Merc. Boetia's continent;
And from (If I shall make a true confession)
Amphitrio's wife, with whom he has congression.

Apoll. With her his courage then and strength he tries
But for his lust will not one night suffice?

Merc. O by no means, since in this copulation
Must be begot one that shall awe each Nation;
Of a most potent arm, and daring much,
And therefore it is not possible that such
A mighty work as making up Jove's son,
Should in one night be perfected and don.

Apollo. Well, I but little have to say unto him,
But with this great work much good may it do him.
These things, o Mercury (we are alone)
In The ancient days of Saturn were not known;
He did not turn from Rhea, nor misled
Could he be to adulterat her chaste bed:
Nor did he leave the heavens, in Thebes to sleep;
The day was then day, and true course did keep,
The night within her certain hours was bounded,
No times, no seasons in his reign confounded:
He had with mortal creatures no congresse.
But now for one poor woman's sake (I guess)
All things are topside-turn'd, and must be made
Prepostrous henceforth, and run retrograde.
My Steeds with rest will grow more fierce and hot:
The way more hard and difficult, because not
In three days past: Men miserably dwell
Here on the earth in darkness, as in hell.
And these are the fair fruits of his foul lust,
That sublunarie creatures suffer must;
Warning at once the absence of the Sun,
and waiting till this mighty work be don.

Merc. Phoebus no more: had Jove intelligence
^f what thou speakst, his rage it would incense.
^e to the Moon and Sleep, and what in charge
^ad from him, deliver them at large:
^o her, to change the course she late did keep:
^o him, to setter them in bonds of sleep,
^o fast, they may not dream of that great wrong,
^o have been kept from sight of day so long.

MERCVRY and MAIA.
The Argument.
HErmes his tedious labors does complain,
As troubled more than all the gods besides,
Not able his employments to sustain,
As one that in no certain place abides.
Yet by his mother he at length is swayd,
Who tells him Jove's bests must be still obayd.
The DIALOGVE.
Merc. IS there amongst the gods (o Mother) any So wretched as my self, though
there be man

Maia. Take heed, my son, what thou spea^ rashly.

Merc. Why?

Can you name one that has such cause as I?
Who have so many businesses in hand,
And those so great I scarce beneath them stand;
Into so many services divided,
I am tired and spent, and for my pains derided.
For in the morning, e're I can devise
Of what my dreams were, I betimes must rise,
Then my first office is to sweep the house
Where all the gods must banquet and carouse
That done, I next prepare the Consistory,
Whereas the Deities in all their glory
^ppoint their meetings: all things I make fit,
That they in ease as well as state may sit.
Then at Jove's elbow I attend, where he
^till sends me on his errands: I must be
^ere, there, and every where, and these too all
^urrying together; for he will sometimes call
^s soon as I am sent. When the whole day
^ have toild, not having time to wipe away
